1. BACKGROUND
A. INTRODUCTION
1.1   The Government of China (GOC) has requested World Bank assistance in
financing a national seed commercialization project. The project would help develop a
conducive environment for the development of a commercial seed sector through the
adoption cf necessary policies and an appropriate regulatory framework. It would
modernize, rationalize and commercialize a number of seed companies and strengthen the
public seed sector so that it can provide effective services to farmers. It would provide
support to several national and provincial seed organizations and would modernize seed
companies from 14 provinces. The project aims ultimately to facilitate the development
of a vibrant seed sector, which can not only provide increased amounts of quality seed to
farmers but also operate through efficient and self-sustaining commercial enterprises.
Incremental benefits would come from improved yield, lower sowing rates and reduced
seed costs for major crops such as hybrid rice, hybrid maize, wheat, soybeans, cotton,
vegetables and fruit trees. This in turn would increase farmer incomes and employment.
The project was identified by a Food and Agriculture Organization/Cooperative Program
(FAO/CP) mission and a World Bank team in November 1993 and prepared in May and
November 1994. It was preappraised and then appraised by World Bank missions in
April 1995 and September/October 1995.
B. AGRICULTURAL SECTOR
1.2   Agriculture continues to play a major role in the Chinese economy. It is estimated
that agriculture accoun s for 20 percent of GNP and provides employment for about 200
million farm families. Less than 15 percent of China's land area is arable, and farming
systems are generaily very intensive with heavy inputs of labor and fertilizer. Nearly half
the cropped area is irrigated. This intensive farming has enabled China to meet the great
bulk of its basic food requirements.
1.3   As a result of policy reforms in the late 1970s-in particular the introduction of
the household production responsibility system and the development of rural markets-
the value of agricultural output grew at an impressive average of 6 percent per year over
the period 1978-1990. The growth rate increased to over 7 percent during 1990-93 but
declined substantially to 2.4 percent in 1994. Grain production, which ranged between
376-402 million tons during 1985-1988, was the Government's central agricultural
concern during the late 1980s. Major efforts were made to increase grain production, and
by the 1990s production had risen to a new plateau of around 450 million tons (435456
million tons between 1990-94).



- 2 -
1.4   In 1994, the Government was concerned about the decline in rice and wheat
output. The official estimates of paddy production were 189 million tons in 1990 but
only 176 millic.n tons in 1994. Likewise, wheat production fell from 106 million ton in
1993 to 99 million tons in 1994. This has resulted in an increase in wheat and rice
imports and a suspension in exports of rice and corn due to insufficient stocks. The
nationwide inflation in 1993-94 combined witH "lagging commodity price management"
placed farmers in a cost/price squeeze that discouraged fertilizer purchases and
contributed-along with the bad weather-to the drop in agriculture production and a
substantial decline in gross value of agricultural output in 1994.  Since then, the
government has improved its monetary policy to stabilize inflation and to maintain
suitable terms of trade for grain production with respect to input prices. Farmers were
able to farm more intensively with improved varieties of seeds, and output has increased.
Grain production was reported to reach another record high of 466 million tons in 1995.
C. BANK GROUP LENDING IN AGRICULTURE
1.5   Bank Group lending to China for agriculture is designed to support the
government's efforts to increase and diversify agricultural production. Specific policy
considerations include reforms designed to make government agencies more efficient and
commercialize enterprises and services. Bank Group lending to date has comprised 43
operations, amounting to more than $6.2 billion and has covered a wide spectrum of
geographic areas and subsectors, with major efforts in land development, irrigation and
drainage, flood control (11 projects), upgrading of agricultural support services including
agricultural research, extension, seed improvement (3 projects), development of
specialized subsectors including forestry, fruit and fisheries (9 projects), agroindustry,
credit, and logistics (8 projects), watershed rehabilitation and poverty alleviation (5
projects) and area development (7 projects). This lending has been designed to support
the govemmeni's efforts to increase and diversify agricultural production through more
efficient use of land, water, capital and labor and through the improvement of support
services such as credit, research, extension and veterinary services.
1.6   Since 1987, the Bank Group has supported a series of provincial development
projects in which an area development approach has been applied. The projects were
designed to alleviate poverty and to assist the Government in its ongoing reform process.
The Rural Seclor Adjustment Loan (Cr. 1932-CHA and Ln. 2967-CHA), which was
approved during 1988, contributed to the articulation of the overall reform process, and
involved the Bank Group in increasingly intensive policy dialogue in agriculture and
poverty allevialion, sustained through the process of project development and several
major sector stLdies (e.g., on grain, poverty alleviation, animal feed, the management of
the Yellow River and marketing of agricultural crops) and project formulation exercises.
1.7   Implementation of most ongoing Bank Group-financed projects in the agriculture
sector is on schedule. Experience in previous Bank Group agricultural projects in China
is that physical works have been completed on time or ahead of schedule. Disbursements
for agricultural projects, in fact, usually outpace those in other sectors. Notable features



- 3 -
of all the projects are the high standard of project management, the active participation of
farmers and the effective collaboration of government authorities at all levels. Sixteen
projects have been completed. The completion reports for these projects indicate that
implementation was generally successful, that most economic rates of return reached or
exceeded appraisal estimates, and that the technology used was sound and appropriate for
wider application.
1.8   The Bank Group's current lending strategy in agriculture, while maintaining
subsectoral development and poverty alleviation objectives, is putting greater emphasis
on commercial development. The new projects under preparation such as the Animal
Feed Project, the State Farms Commercialization Project and this project, all aim at
strengthening the commercial orientation of these subsectors and enabling future private-
sector participation.
D. SEED SUBSECTOR
1.9   Advances in seed technology are one of the most important channels for
improving productivity in agriculture. The government recognizes the crucial role of the
seed industry in achieving national targets of grain production and agriculture production
goals generally. For this reason, it sees as a major constraint the present structure of the
seed sector, which allows low-productivity, low-quality, unresponsive, loss-making seed
producers to persist. There is full recognition of the need for major steps in the direction
of commercialization, if only to stop the drain on the budget from the need to cover seed
company losses. Central government strategies, while ensuring seed supply to farmers,
will also promote efficient seed commercialization. At the macro level, the Government
aims to create a conducive environment to support the seed commercial development by
drafting policy and regulatory reforms. Its policies are designed to promote seed-sector
commercialization by shifting from an administrative hierarchy of seed companies
(provincial, prefecture and county) to a competitive structure and by supporting efficient
companies. At the enterprise level, it emphasizes high-quality and new-variety seeds,
modernization of seed processing and packaging equipment and seed enterprise
profitability. The success of commercialization strategies will be influenced by overall
economic reform in China. In particular, it will be influenced by the overall reform
program for state-owned enterprises (SOEs), since the sector is now dominated by
jurisdictional seed companies and state farms.
1.10 The formal seed sector in China is large and complex. In 1993 there were more
than 2,700 seed companies in China, one for almost every administrative division within
the country. The companies are loosely networked in a pyramidal structure with the
China National Seed Corporation (CNSC) at the apex. Beneath the CNSC, each of the 30
provinces has a provincial seed company, and below these are some 500 prefecture and
2,200 county seed companies. All the seed companies are state-owned enterprises known
as executive/business management units. In addition there are 2,500 Township Seed
Supply Stations (TSSS) owned by farmer collectives, whose main function is to distribute
seeds to farmers. They are also responsible for organizing the production and local sale



- 4 -
of conventional (i.e., nonhybrid) seed of crops such as rice and wheat by farmer
cooperatives.
1.11   The uset of seed produced by the organized sector has increased from about 1.3
million tons in 1976 to over 3.5 million tons in 1994. This is about 25 percent of the total
annual seed requirement of about 12.5 million tons. The balance is met by the informal
seed sector, i.e. by farmers' own seed or farmer-to-farmer sales. Given that conventional
crops, such as wheat and self-pollinated rice, make up the bulk of crop production and
that much of the area under these crops does not need new seed every year, the ratio of
processed to farmer seed is reasonable. It is officially projected that in the year 2000 the
total demand For seed will be 12.0 million tons (the reduction being due to reduced
seeding rates and a reduction in the total sown area) and that the organized sector will
supply about 44 percent of this demand.
1.12  In addition to the seed companies, a large number of state farms (SFs) and a few
collective seed companies are also involved in the production of seed. While most SF
seed production is for own use, a nunber of farms specialize in industrial crop seed
production (e.g., sugar beet, cotton or oilseed rape) for sale to other state farms. At
present there are a veiy limrited number of private seed companies due both to the general
difficulty in establishing private sector enterprises and also to specific regulatory
requirements in the seed sector.
1.13   1 he organization of agricultural seed activities in China can be summarized as
follows:
(a)    Selection and Breeding: The selection and breeding of varieties is mainly
done at agricultural research and educational institutions such as agricultural
science academies and agricultural universities. Some seed companies are
increasingly giving emphasis to selecting and breeding their own varieties.
There are no restrictions in this area, and individuals and collective entities
are also encouraged to carry out breeding.
(b)    Adaptation and Regional Trials:  Trials are jointly organized by the
national and provincial agricultural research institutions and seed
administration stations in the provinces. Any new promising line has to
un-crgo tv. o to three years regional adaptability trials and one to two years
field production trials for consideration for registration and approval for
release.
(c)    Variety Examination:  Evaluation of trial results is done at national,
provincial or prefectural/municipality level by the respective crop variety
examination committees, which comprise members from local governments,
seed administrations, and research and academic institutions. A promising
candidate line, which possesses definite quality advantages over existing
varieties, will be awarded a variety certificate, registered and announced by
the agricultural department at the corresponding administrative level.



- 5 -
(d)   Seed Production: Seed production is mainly managed by county seed
companies and state fanns and is done in their own seed production bases.
Early generations are produced in state stock seed farms. Hybrids of
important crops are only produced by government designated companies.
Townships and individuals also produce seed in their seed production bases
mainly for use in their own administrative areas.
(e)   Seed Marketing and Distribution: Seed companies are still the main
marketing channels in China, and hybrids of important crops are marketed
only by the designated companies that produce them. Conventional seed
and vegetable seed are marketed through different channels, and collectives
and individuals are allowed to trade in these seeds. The basic requirement
for marketing seed is to obtain a seed business operation permit from the
local seed station and the permit in turn is used to obtain a business
operation license issued by the local Industrial and Commercial
Administration office. Since 1992, the Ministry of Agriculture, through
regulations on producing and marketing crop seed and animal vaccines by
agricultural, scientific, research and educational units, has pemitted research
and educational institutions to produce and market all kinds of crop seed.
(f)   Seed Quality Inspection and Quarantine:  Seed quality inspection is
enforced by the Department of Agriculture seed administration stations at
the county level and above. Quarantine inspection is done by the
Administration of Animal and Plant Quarantine Services at its 280 border
and port/airport district and subdistrict stations in all the provinces and
autonomous regions.
Seed Industry Structure
1.14  Seed companies are primarily responsible to government agencies at their
administrative levels. Thus the China National Seed Corporation is responsible to the
Department of Agriculture (DOA) of the Ministry of Agriculture, the Provincial Seed
Companies (PSCs) are responsible to the provincial Departments of Agriculture, and
similarly at prefecture, municipal and county levels.
1.15 The lines of command and responsibility for the state farms vary somewhat from
province to province, but at the national level, the Bureau of State Farms within MOA
has overall responsibility. An organogram indicating the organizational structure of the
seed industry and its position within MOA is provided in Figure 1.
Regulatory Agencies And Institutions
1.16 MOA has four main agencies concemed directly with seed regulations and policy,
as follows:



- 6 -
(a)   DOA Seed Division (SD): This unit is responsible for drafting seed laws,
regulations, plans and policies and for supervising seed management.
(h)   National Agricultural Technical Extension Service Center (NATESC):
The National Seed Administration Station and the Plant Protection Station
became part of a new National Agricultural Technical Extension Service
Center (NATESC). Seed activities are grouped into three divisions within
the center-the Seed Industry Management Division, the Seed Testing
Division (which contains the National Seed Testing Center) and the Variety
Trial and Seed Multiplication Division. These Divisions are responsible for
the implementation of seed extension and national seed security, application
of new technologies and methods of seed production and seed processing,
control and regulation of the seed industry and seed quality, and vocational
training. The provincial, prefectural and county seed administration stations,
however, have not been reorganized as part of their respective extension
services.
(c)   National Crop Variety Examination and Approval Committee Office
(NCVEACO):  This unit is responsible for coordinating, supervising,
examining and registering new varieties at the national level. Registration of
new crop varieties at the provincial level is carried out by the provincial
CVEACO.
(d)   Adwinistration of Animal and Plant Quarantine Services (AAPQS):
This is anothler MOA executing unit that is responsible for quarantine in the
international exchange of plants and animals.
1.17  Ir. addition, the National Patent Office through the patent law is responsible for
the prot'ction of new plant varieties by pa.enting their breeding methodologies.
Apvlications for patent are coordinated by the Patent Division of the MOA Department of
Science, Technology and Quality Standardization in Beijing.
Seed Companies
1.18 China National Seed Corporation (CNSC). CNSC was incorporated into MOA
in 1982 with responsibility for planning, coordinating and distributing domestic seed
production and the import and export of all seed. CNSC is an integrated company,
involved in research, production and trade with more than 40 subsidiaries and joint
ventures. The main seeds produced by these in eight provinces are: vegetables (200 tons
in 1993), melon, hybrid maize (150,000 tons) and rice (2,000 tons), cotton, jute, oilseeds,
herbs and flowers. It owns 46 retail outlets in China and has trading arrangements with
more than 80 clients in 40 countries worldwide. In 1992, under the market reform
program, CNSC became an autonomous seed trading company. It no longer has
responsibility for administrative planning of domestic seed production, nor does it have a
monopoly on exports and imports. It remains involved in seed policy-making at the
national level and in the coordination of the seed trade among the provinces.



1.19  Provincial Seed Companies (PSCs). In general the PSCs are responsible for
preparing annual provincial seed development prograns and plans, which provide
guidance for lower-level seed companies. Until recently they were also exclusively able
to undertake interprovincial trade in seeds. The PSCs concentrate on the multiplication
of breeder seed obtained from research academies or universities. Breeding material,
previously supplied free, is now paid for either by outright purchase or by the payment of
royalties. Some PSCs have established joint ventures with overseas companies to
produce special high-value seeds, usually horticultural crops for export.
1.20 Prefectural/Municipal Seed Companies (MSCs). The main responsibility of
the MSCs is to produce foundation seed from the breeder seed supplied by the PSCs.
Most MSCs also produce breeder seed, production or certified seed, and many specialize
in producing seed of particular crops. Seed is produced on production bases selected and
supervised by the MSCs. A considerable number of MSCs have processing plants to
clean, grade and pack seeds.
1.21   County Seed Companies (CSCs). These are the backbone of the Chinese seed
industry, being responsible for the production of certified seed from foundation seed
supplied by the MSCs. Commercial seed is actually produced by farmers under contract
to Village Committees, which are usually organized into production bases by
agrotechnical extension stations (ATES). Contracts detail the price to be paid for the
seed, the quantity to be supplied, the procurement time and penalties for failure to meet
requirements. CSCs are responsible for ensuring the supply of inputs (foundation seed,
maternal and paternal parent seed for hybrids, fertilizer and pesticides). Inputs are
generally supplied on credit, but at full cost. However in some cases such as fertilizer for
hybrid rice seed, the price is subsidized. The CSCs provide technical advice to growers
and are responsible for monitoring the seed crop for certification purposes. CSCs
undertake seed processing and testing according to the standards and quality of their
facilities.
1.22  State Farm Seed Companies. State farm seed companies are usually subfarms
or teams within the administration of state farms, most lacking independent financial
accounts. Normally, these seed producers have no official relationship with seed
companies under the agiicultural system. However, they also sell their surplus seed in
nearby farm markets. Specialty seed may be supplied to a much wider area.
1.23  Informal Seed Sector. It is reported that about 20-25 percent of the seeds
produced in China for self-pollinating crops of conventional varieties are obtained from
seed companies/extension stations while the rest (75-80 percent) are seeds saved by
farmers for planting the next crop. Except for local specialty crops, there appears to be
virtually no direct marketing of seeds by farmers-production is either strictly for own
use or umder contractuai arrangements with the seed companies.
1.24  Private Seed Sector. Private seed companies do not really exist at present. A
few county seed companies and research institutes have spun off snall subsidiaries that



- 8 -
produce seed of high value crops such as hybrid grain seeds, vegetables and horticulture
crops and have an ownership structure that includes some private parties, mostly through
worker sharehiolding. Almost all have other activities in addition to producing and
marketing seeds, and most are profitable. The number and size of these companies will
probably increase in the future due to continuing increase in the demand for vegetables
and thus vegetable seeds. The area under vegetable production is now estimated at 4.5
million ha or 3 percent of the area under crops, requiring about 1.2 million tons of
vegetable seed each year.
E. SEED TECHNOLOGY AND PERFORMANCE
1.25   Rice.  In the 1970s, the Chinese Government searched for solutions to the
prevailing food grain shortages that had resulted from increasing population and stagnant
grain yield. Rice, wheat and corn were chosen for research and development due to their
potential for increased yields. The first hybrid rice was identified in 1974, and by 1975,
hybrid rice was ready to be released for commercial production. The parent lines were
from the best selected local varieties and imported ones from the International Rice
Research Institute (IRRI) and Thailand. Initially, the average seed yield of hybrid rice
seed farms was low (0.4 tiha) but by 1991 the average yield of seed was 2.25 t/ha. In
some areas, it has reached 4-5 t/ha.' The great improvement in hybrid rice seed
production was due to the development of efficient seed production methods, improved
parent lines and a good production system. The Government has used its information
systems to create awareness of the existence and benefits of hybrid seed among farmers.
Responsibility was assigned to one or more officials at each level to monitor the progress
of adoption of hybrid rice, which was promoted by training and extension.
Demonstration plots were emphasized.
1.26  The farm yield of hybrid rice increased from 4.2 t/ha in 1976 to 5.5 t/ha in 1991.
The area under hybrid rice increased from 0.1 million ha in 1976 to 15.4 million ha in
1991, representing 45 percent of the total rice production area. The government plans to
increase the area under hybrid rice by another 10 percent by the year 2000 and to increase
the farm yield to 6-7.5 tlha. To achieve this high yield, China will need new germplasm
from international sources.
1.27 Wheat is the second major grain crop after rice, occupying about 29 million ha
per year with a total output of 99 million tons in 1994. Spring wheat, accounting for 17
percent of the total area, is planted in March-April and harvested in July and is largely a
single crop. ][t is grown in the north of China in Heilongjiang, Jilin, Liaoning, Inner
Mongolia, Gansu, Xinjiang and other provinces. Winter wheat, accounting for 83 percent
of the sown wheat area, is planted in late September and harvested in mid-June.
Average seed yields are always lower than the grain yields of farns using that seed, inter alia, due to the need to
reject much unpure seed production.



-9-
Depending on the availability of irrigation water, double cropping of wheat followed by
com, millet, beans and other cash crops is practiced. Production of winter wheat is
concentrated in 5 zones: North China, Upper Yellow River, Yangtze River, Southwest,
and South Chine.
1.28  Wheat yields increased moderately, from 2.8 t/ha in 1984 to 3.4 t/ha in 1994,
because of improved varieties of seed, which are early maturing and have better
resistance to disease, drought and cold. For example, cold resistant varieties (Fengkang
and CA837) have marginally improved yields in the northern region (21 percent over the
last 10-year period). But the development of still better varieties through research and
development should be a priority because wheat imports have been increasing due to
increases in domestic demand and lagging production.
1.29 Corn is the third main grain of China, after rice and wheat. In 1994, the corn
sown area was 21 million hectares (18.7 percent of the country's grain sown area), and
total corn production was 99 million tons, accounting for 22.3 percent of the country's
total grain production of 445 million tons. Most of the production is located in the north
and northeast regions, which has 70 percent of the sown area and 85 percent of total
production. The country's average corn yield is 4.7 t/ha, 15 percent higher than the
average grain yield of 4.1 t/ha
1.30 The increase in China's corn production has mainly been due to improved hybrid
varieties, such as Dan Yu and Yie Tan, which have "compact" seed. The development of
hybrid corn began in the 1950s. Double cross was introduced in the 1960s and single
cross in the 1970s. The development and popularization of compact hybrid corn seed
alone resulted in a yield increment of 1.5 t/ha. In 1965, hybrid com accounted for about
10 percent of the com sown area, and the average yield was 1 tlha. The area sown to
hybrid corn progressively expanded to 79 percent by 1975 and 90 percent by 1994, and
the corn yield increased to 3.6 t/ha in 1975 and 4.7 ttha in 1994. The average yield
differs significantly by region, ranging from 5.3 t/ha in the northem region (Jilin,
Liaoning and Heilongjiang) to 3 t/ha in the southem region (Sichuan, Yunnan and
Guizhou). Corn breeders believe that average yields could be increased to 6.5 t/ha, if
they could get arctcss to improved breeding materials. The aim is to produce seeds with
high but stable yield and disease and pest tolerance. Better water supplies and inputs
would aiso contribute to increased production.



- 10-
2. SEED SECTOR DEVELOPMENT
A. HISTORICAL BACKGROUND
2.1   After the establishment of the People's Republic, food production was the
principal concern of the Chinese Government and seed development was given priority.
In 1949 the Government established a Seed Division within the Ministry of Food to plan
and manage tho supply of seed in the country. The separate Ministry of Agriculture
(MOA) was given the responsibility for variety evaluation and recommendation, and the
results were transferred to the Food Ministry for implementation. In 1958, due to the lack
of seed technicians in the Food Ministry, all responsibilities for seed, encompassing
administrative, techlical and business functions, were transferred to MOA.
2.2  In 1978, the China National Seed Corporation (CNSC) was set up to plan,
coordinate and distribute domestic seed and handle overseas dealings. It was also known
as the National Seed Bureau. Seed stations, which had the combined responsibilities of
seed administrations and seed companies, were also set up. In 1980, the State Council
issued a decree to separate the administrative functions of the CNSC from its business
activities. The separation was achieved in 1982, resulting in the establishment of the
MOA Seed Division (SD) and the National Seed Administration Station (NSAS). In
1991 the National Crop Variety Examination and Approval Committee Office
(NCVEACO) was formed to be responsible for new variety evaluation.
2.3   The development of the Chinese seed industry can be broadly divided into three
phases: (a) the period 1950-1977 when all seed sector activities were service oriented
and profit was not to be mentioned; (b) the period 1978-1989, when seed companies were
established, allowed to make minimal profits, and encouraged to produce seeds in which
they had a cornparative advantage; and (c) the period 1990-present, which marks the
beginning of seed market liberalization. Seed market liberalization started with removal
of CNSC monopoly power, the freeing up of the market for vegetable seed, the relaxation
of seed prices and the toleration of seed trade across political boundaries and
intemationally.
B. CONSTRAINTS
2.4   Policy, :regulatory, technical and financial factors present major inhibitions to the
growth in the seed sector, as follows.
Policy and Regulatory Constraints
2.5   The Secd Administration Regulations of May 1989 contain administrative policy
guidelines forrmulated to strengthen the development of seed activities by insuring



..ppropriate seed quality standards and safeguarding the rights and interests of seed
breeders, distributors and users in a centrally planned economy. The regulations contain
policies on germplasm management, plant variety registration, strategic seed reserve
management and quarantine. Government considers they are inadequate for supporting
the new policy to develop a commercial seed sector. In particular, they do not deal with
issues of domestic trade across political boundaries, truth in labeling, distribution, breeder
rights, farmers rights, liability and compensation, and research institutions.
2.6   Plant Breeder Rights. At present, China has no legislation to protect intellectual
property rights in plant cultivars. Without such protection, it is currently very difficult
for breeders to sustain themselves financially in the current climate of diminishing
government subsidies for new cultivar development. The 1992 patent law provides some
measure of protection for hybrid seed. This law protects the breeding methods and
techniques used to produce new cultivars, but not the cultivars thernselves. In the case of
hybrid seed, users must return to the seed supplier for each growing season, and where
the availability of parent lines is tightly controlled, the patentability of the breeding
process affords protection to the cultivar. For open pollinated species such as wheat,
however, it is generally not possible for the breeder to gain adequate financial
remuneration after the initial release of seed, since farmers may produce seed for their
own use without consideration of ownership.
2.7   The lack of plant cultivar protection has discouraged foreign breeders from
introducing new germplasm from outside China. It is impossible to quantify the lost
opportunity represented by lack of access to the latest germplasm, but it has been
estimated by scientists ftom a multinational seed company that yields of strategically
important crops such as corn could be increased by as much as 15 percent if new
germplasm is introduced from outside China.
2.8   Seed Testing. The testing of seed for the physical attributes of germination,
purity and moisture content is a pivotal procedure in the management of seed quality.
MOA has acknowledged the requirement for seed testing within its regulatory framework
through promulgation of national seed standards and the establishment of a national seed
testing laboratory and associated laboratories at provincial and lower levels. In practice,
however, most of the seed laboratories are inadequately equipped and staff poorly trained.
The rules and procedures utilized by laboratories are outdated and do not reflect modem
knowledge in the area of seed science. The Bank-assisted Agricultural Support Services
Project (ASSP, Cr. 2462-CHA) (para. 2.31) is currently addressing some of these
deficiencies, and the present project would do considerably more.
2.9   Since 1991, China has been a member of the International Seed Testing
Association (ISTA), the primary international organization concerned with the
development and promulgation of seed testing methods. China's membership has been
through the Bureau of Animal Husbandry with the Forage Seed Laboratory of Beijing
Agricultural University being the sole accredited member laboratory. The National Seed
Testing Laboratory is currently not a member of ISTA.



- 12 -
2.10   Seed Certification. Most countries have developed programs for the certification
of the genetic integrity of plant cultivars within their seed regulatory framework. These
programs associated with the production of seed, essentially maintain an evidentiary trail
on the seed of cultivars as it is multiplied through successive generations. They are an
assurance to seed users that the cultivar stated on a label is true to type. At present China
does not have a reguilated seed certification scheme, as cultivar assurance has been the
responsibility of government operated seed companies, and this process has operated
satisfactorily. As the seed industry becomes more deregulated in the future, particularly
if seed companies cease to be state-owned enterprises, it may be necessary to have a
v oluntary seed certification scheme available in the public interest. However, this is not
Seen as an immediate necessity.
2.11   Most, if not all seed exported from China at present is of vegetable species, for
which seed certification is not required internationally. Should seed of other species, e.g.,
corn, be exported, China may need to conform to the OECD Seed Scheme for the
certification of seed in international trade, since importing countries usually require
internationally acceptable certification procedures. While possibly useful in the future,
the development of seed certification programs for either internal or external markets is
not seen as a current constraint on the development of the seed sector.
2.12 Crop V7ariety Examination, Evaluation, Approval and Registration. China
has had a formal system for the examination, evaluation, release and registration of crop
varieties for more than 30 years, now under the authority of Articles 12 and 13 of the
1989 Seed Regulations. All crop varieties developed in China or imported have to be
examined, evaluated, approved and registered before the seeds or vegetative propagules
can be multiplied and marketed to farmers. This system has the hierarchical structure of
most institutionls in the agricultural sector: NCVEACO coordinates and supervises the
examination and approval of new crop varieties at the national level with assistance of 14
professional crop-group subcommittees and the Provincial Crop Variety Evaluation and
Approval Corrumittees have responsibility for the examination and approval of crop
varieties at the provincial level also typically with the assistance of 14 provincial expert
subcommittees.
2.13   Under this two-tier examination, evaluation and approval system, the application
for release and approval of a new crop variety is usually made at the provincial level with
testing/examination in provincial trials for one year before it is entered into national
trials. A minimum of two years in provincial regional trials (PRT) for distinctness,
uniformity and stability and two years in provincial field production trials (PFPT) for
crop value and p:oductivity are generally required before a decision is made on provincial
relea:e ind the same for national approval, i.e., two years national regional trials (NRT)
and two years national field production trials (NFPT). Since the PFPT and the NRT
ornmence after the first PRT, the time to approval and registration of new and/or
imported crop varieties is three to four years at the provincial level and four to six years
at the national level. Varieties approved and registered for national usage carry a GS
prefix. Variety names are assigned by the NCVEACO.



- 13 -
2.14  Although they are very formal and very conservative, the crop variety
examination, evaluation and registration system and requirements have not been viewed
as a significant constraint on either crop productivity or seed industry development until
rather recently. The present view, however, is that with increasing emphasis on the
adoption of advanced technologies to increase crop productivity coinciding with the
commercialization of the seed industry, the present system will now be a significant
impediment to increased agricultural productivity unless it is substantially reformed and
liberalized. An example cited is an important and much needed IRRI rice variety that
took four years for registration. Reforms especially needed include shortening of the
period for approval and registration, relaxation of cropping area restrictions and
permission for breeders to name varieties. Additionally, NCVEACO is ill-equipped to
carry out its assigned role. Among other things, it needs: (a) a modem computerized
information system to record, analyze and store the voluminous data on provincially and
nationally registered varieties, so that crop variety catalogs and databases can be
efficiently assembled, and (b) an adequate temperature- and humidity-controlled seed
store to maintain samples of seeds under trial prior to their being stored in perpetuity with
the National Institute of Germplasm Resources.
2.15   Prices and Trade. Until 1992, procurement and selling prices of improved seeds
were decided by provincial seed corporations within a price range suggested by the
provincial DOA and the provincial price bureau, which in turn was based on prices
established for commodiity grains. In principle, the seed prices were set at 10-15 percent
more than the prevailing free market grain price. Thus, seed prices did not reflect the
costs actually involved in producing and marketing seeds. A second level of controls
affecting seed prices comes from government limitation of the profits that state
enterprises can make from seed operations. Since 1992, there has been considerable
liberalization of seed prices. In many cases seed prices are now determined by the
market. For instance, hybrid corn seed is usually now about three times the commercial
corn price, and hybrid rice six times. However, in some provinces the prices of these
seeds are still constrained to be only 10-15 percent above the commercial price.
2.16   Until recently trade was limited to within a seed company's jurisdiction, typically
a county, but in the case of a provincial seed company, the province. Interprovincial and
even inter-county trade was small and occurred mostly under specific government
instructions. Official regulations also restricted trading and marketing of seed to seed
companies. It llow appears that there are no longer any enforced controls on trade
between provinces. 1Nevertheless, a number of restrictions remain, except in times of
shortage: (a) only designated companies, invariably state or provincial companies, can
distribute hybrid seeds for the rmain crops, (b) most provinces insist that seed produced in
anc-her province is sold only through the importing province's provincial seed company,
(c) interprovincial imports are frequently subject to a levy of up to 10 percent ad valorem,
(d) there are a large number of permits required to establish a seed company, and
(e) the present system of certification and seed control discourages competition. Freedom
to enter or exit the market, or a sector of a market, is essential, if farmers are to be given



- 14 -
the choice of ivhere to purchase their seeds. This is being addressed under the new seed
law (para. 2.22).
2.17   State-0)v ;aed Enterprises.  A major constraint for the development of a
comr.e_ciaii.cd seed sector is the fact that all seed producers are state owned enterprises
(SOEs) with all the ramifications that this entails. SOEs typically receive their orders
From the state system and, as government services, do not compete with each other. The
seed companies are now in an ambiguous situation. On the one hand, they are expected
to continue to play a social role by providing farmers with quality seed at set, minimum
prices; on the other, they are now expected to become commercial. In general, they are
still more concerned with the volume of output to meet state requirements than with
profit generation and sustainable investment. As a further aspect of their status as SOEs,
they remain obliged to provide housing, health, education and pension services for staffs
that are usually much larger than actual labor need.
Technical Cornstraints
2.18   There are two major technical constraints for the development of a modern
commercialized seed industry. Once recognized, they can only be addressed by major
investment. The first technical constraint is the lack of sufficiently developed seed
production bases. Quality seed production has to be done in seed production bases to
achieve proper isolation, uniform crop management and efficient operation.
Unfortunately, because farms in China are small (averaging 0.1 ha/capita), organizing
efficient production bases composed of individual farmers is difficult.  Thus, seed
companies desiring to enter the market for quality seed will typically plan to develop
production bases on tracts that they manage. In this regard, state farms, with readily
available plots, will have an advantage. In all cases, the development of quality
production bases will require significant investment and upgraded production technology.
2.19  The lack of seed testing laboratoiies equipped with full-time seed testing
personnel and seed testing equipment is another major technical constraint to improved
seed quality. A shortage of modern, efficient processing equipment and facilities and a
sLonage of manpower trained in modern seed production regulation and control
procedures is a second major technical constraint to seed quality.  Together these
deficiencies make it very difficult for most seed companies to produce and market seed of
good physical and genetic quality. The experience of companies that have been re-
equipped with modem facilities demonstrates the benefits to be obtained from the use of
efficient processing facilities (threshers, driers, cleaners, graders, delinters, chemical
applicators, and packaging equipment etc.). A prerequisite for the modernization of the
industry is therefore a major program of investment in new machinery and the related
training of opeirating staff.
Finarcial Constraints
2.20  Most seed enterprises appear to be affected by a shortage of working capital for
the operation of production bases (i.e. annual purchase of inputs for supply to farmer-



- 15-
p.-oducers) and for the purchase of raw seed. An analysis of the typical cost structure of a
provincial seed companay in 1994 indicates that during peak periods purchasing of raw
seed represents some 80 percent of the capital requirements of a seed company.
2.21   Most enterprises also appear to find it difficult to raise adequate amounts of
investment capital for the purchase of new equipment, especially for foreign exchange for
imported machinery, and for the improvement of production bases (e.g., irrigation and
drainage facilities, and access roads). At the most basic level, the firms' financial
difficulties are a reflection of the limited ability they have to generate and retain surplus
revenue over and above immediate financial needs. The margin between the sale price of
processed seed and the procurement price paid to farmers is insufficient to cover
processing costs in fill, let alone generate sufficient profit to finance reinvestment.
Analysis of seed company proposals submitted for inclusion under this project indicated
that only about one third were viable on the basis of 1994 selling prices. The long term
viability of a commercial seed industry will depend on improved access to short and long
termn capital, rationalization of the industry leading to fewer but more competitive
enterprises, and fiL-'her liberalization of prices (para. 2.15) to allow adequate profit
mar gin-.
C. RECENT DEVELOPMENTS
Poliky and Regulatory Framework
2.22   Seed Policy. China's overall seed policy aims to increase the use of improved
agricuitLiral and horticultural seeds in order to increase the country's food and fiber
production. Policks to promote seed-sector commercialization include shifting from an
administrative hierarchy of seed companies (provincial, prefecture and county) to a
competitive structure and to support efficient companies.
2.23   To achieve this goal the following seed sectoral objectives are under consideration
by the government for the year 2000:
(a)    Law drafting. To prepare and enact "the Seed Law of China" and "New
Plant Variety Protection Regulations" and detailed implementing particulars,
including participation in the Intemational Union for the Protection of New
Varieties of Plants (UPOV) Convention;
(b)   Separation of the administrative and business functions of existing
administrative seed companies. To create an independent official seed
administration and monitoring system to regulate the seed industry and to
promote the establishment of modem seed corporations;
(z)    Replacement of cereal varieties. To replace present varieties of main
cereals such as rice, wheat and corn with new varieties to firther increase
yield by 10 percent;



- 16 -
(d)   Increase in the business volume of state-owned companies. To increase
the business volume of state-owned companies from 3.5 mil tons to 5.0 mil
tons; and
(e)   Improvement in seed quality. To achieve at least the usage of national
class 2 seeds in all field crops.
2.24   Seed Legislation. Certain areas of seed law are to be amplified and strengthened,
while other aspects of the seed sector are to be deregulated. The overall effect will be to
facilitate comrnercial trade on the one hand and tighten official control of seed quality on
the other. The changes proposed by Government would have the effect of:
(a)    Simplifying the procedures for examination and approval of varieties for
cultivation and use and upgrading of the registration system;
(b)   Providing for intellectual property protection for plant cultivars;
(c)    Strengthening official inspection as a quality monitoring tool of the
government to ensure user protection;
(d)   Ending the current seed production monopoly and strengthening of seed
production contract between seed companies and farmers; and
(z)    Strengthening the requirement of marketers to label and package seeds in
accordance with prescribed standards.
2.25   Crop 'Variety Examination, Evaluation, Approval and Registration. Reforms
under consideration would aim at: (a) speeding up procedures at the provincial level with
automatic registration of provincially approved varieties in the National Registry;
(b) reducing t!.e minimum time required for the introduction of new varieties of strategic
crops to two years; (c) eliminating any distinction in terms of procedures or requirements
for domestic or imported varieties; (d) reducing the time required for most vegetable
crops, omamentals and other nonstrategic crops to a maximum of one year for
observation trials; and (e) permitting breeders of new varieties to name them.
2.26  Plant Breeder Rights. In September 1994, a series of seminars were organized
in China in ccnjunction with the International Union for the Protection of New Varieties
of Plants (UPOV) to discuss the legislative framework of intellectual property protection
for plants in the international context. In April 1995, a committee with members from
MOA, the Miinistry of Forestry and the Patent Bureau began drafting legislation for plant
breeder rights protection. The text of an initial draft was reviewed by an official from
UPOV in October 1995 to ensure conformity with the 1978 Act.
2.27  Seed Testing. The former National Seed Administration Station completed a
translation of the latest International Seed Testing Association (ISTA) rules and
procedures into (Chinese in 1994 as a prelude to strengthening seed quality testing.



- 17 -
Manuals containing these rules and procedures were printed in early 1995 and officially
released on August 18, 1995 and will be widely distributed to seed companies and official
seee quality control laboratories. The NATESC laboratory is anticipating the completion
of new laboratory facilities in 1996 under the ASSP, after which time it will be in a
position to join ISTA.
2.28  Seed Production. 'The Government plans to extend seed production authorization
to allow production outside the breeding location, to allow foreign seed companies to
produce in China and to allow for contract seed multiplication for re-export. In times of
high seed denrand, contracts for seed production have often been broken by farmers,
causing trade problems for seed companies. The new seed law would attempt to address
this issue.
2.29  Seed Marketing. Currently, only seed companies are allowed to market seed
(except for farmer-to-farmer trade which is allowed). The Government proposes revision
that would allow any appropriately qualified body to market seed. A license will still be
required. The requirements for labeling and packaging of seed will be strengthened with
greater adherence to truthful labeling principles. Consumer information on labels would
be required. These provisions are directed at increasing the responsibility of the seed
marketing sector for product quality.
D. LESSONS LEARNED FROM PREvIOus BANK GROUP PROJECTS
2.30 The Seeds Project (Cr. 1577-CHA), which was completed in 1990, provided
modern seed processing equipment and trained staff in sced processing, testing and
certification at 18 seed centers. The project was successful in establishing modem
processing facilities and expanding the capacity of some 80 seed production bases. The
project was less successful in introducing modern methods of seed quality assurance and
control procedures. The Project Management Office (PMO) established under the project
to supervise project implementation across 12 provinces and 2 autonomous regions was
very effective. The most important lessons were in the area of marketing and pricing as
follows:
(a)   Greater attention should have been given to estimating market demand for
the project's seed output. Estimates for conventional, self-pollinated grain
and oil seeds were too optimistic, resulting in substantial quantities of
unsold seed, which were disposed of as grain or feed. The shortfall in
demand occurred in part because, with increased freedom of action, farmers
used more of their own rather than purchased seed, and additionally because
of shifts in cropping patterns away from grain and oil seed crops.
(b)   The project should have provided for adequate processing margins based on
a full analysis of costs. The controlled seed prices anticipated by the project
were set at levels that in actual operation did not allow seed companies to
cover costs. Production of quality seed, made possible by the superior
equipment provided under the project, required among other things, a costly



- 18-
5-15 percent seed reject rate. Controlled prices for quality seed needed to be
set higher; or prices should have been liberalized.
2.31 The Agricultural Support Services Project (ASSP-Cr. 2462-CHA), which
became effective in 1993, assists in upgrading the facilities of 10 provincial, 14
prefecture and 41 county seed companies, in strengthening the plant quarantine system
ani in establishing a seed quality control system that is independent of seed companies.
It is significant that t.e Ministry of Agriculture's (MOA) current policy is that in the
future all provinces will be expected to establish external quality control systems that are
independent of szed companies.  It also contains a small pilot program  of
cor1;mercialization of three seed companies. A recent review of these model seed
companies revealed that they have become financially independent entities (although the
ownership of capital assets has yet to be resolved within the framework of State owned
enterprises) and have restructured their operations to take advantage of commercial
opportunities. The companies are doing very well. In all three cases, the Government
has provided budgetary financing for the seed quality control agencies.
2.32  Under the government policy at the time the ASSP was initiated, both seed
company management and regulation operated within a strict hierarchical structure with
the national agencies determining policy and the provinces conducting operations. The
system clearly did not allow for independent monitoring of the quality of the seed
reaching the market and therefore was an unsatisfactory procedure. Under the ASSP, the
Government agreed to institute policies requiring the separation of official seed quality
control from the commercial activities of the seed companies by transferring staff and
budget from the seed company structure to government regulatory agencies at provincial,
prefectural and county level in the target provinces. Financing was made available
through the Project to equip these regulatory agencies with vehicles and laboratory
equipment to operate an efficient and effective monitoring system.
2.33  Full implementation of the ASSP has not yet been completed and a recent review
revealed certain lessons learned in the management and implementation of the project
that will need to be applied in the proposed project as follows: (a) training delay can
affect project progress; and (b) delay in procurement of laboratory equipment can
severely curtail quality control activities and delay the separation of quality control from
production.
2.34 The Henan ADP (Cr. 2242-CHA), which became effective in August 23, 1991,
supported the construction of 20 seed processing centers to produce, process and market
good quality wheat, rice, corn and soybean seeds to meet existing and additional demand
in the project area. The centers are now in operation. Lessons learned from this project
have been used in designing this project, as follows: (a) the processing plants should be
close to the production base; (b) for efficient operation and convenient relocation, the
processing plants should be prefabricated; (c) the quality of seed stores is important in
keeping seeds in good condition; (d) adequate equipment technical specifications are
essential (to ensure that the equipment will incorporate new technology and that a service



-19-
contract, a supply of spare parts, installation and training of operators are included in the
bid package); and (e) processing lines should be procured as a single procurement
package from a single supplier to ensure that the responsibility for satisfactory operation
remains with o-.e company.
2.35  The Seed Development Project (SDP) (Ln. 3891-UA) in Ukraine, effective in
1995, also incorporates some relevant lessons learned in other Bank Group projects in,
among others, Indonesia, India and Pakistan. It aims to improve agricultural productivity
through increasing the supply of high-quality seeds for corn, sugar beets and sunflowers.
It would support the development of private seed production enterprises, improve the
regulatory framework and support germplasm maintenance and improvement. Lessons
reflected in this project are: (a) the ability of seed companies to operate independently is
crucial to private seed sector development; (b) price and trade liberalization are essential
to plomote seed trade and international cooperation; (c) technical assistance is required
for introdu_ing new management systems and preparing operating plans; (d) maintenance
and improvement of germplasm benefit from a research and development program and
from income generation from licensing of new varieties. These lessons have been taken
into consideration while preparing the proposed project.
E. RATIONALE FOR BANK GROUP INVOLVEMENT
2.36  The Country Assistance Strategy (CAS) presented to the Board in June 1995
commits the Bank Group to assisting China to implement market-oriented reforms,
particularly the reform and restructuring of state enterprises. The proposed project would
support that strategy and enterprise restructuring by transferring technical and managerial
know-how to seed enterprises, which would also benefit from increased managerial and
fmancial autonomy. Involvement of the Bank Group in the project would, in addition,
allow it to contribute its experience to key aspects of the modemization and
commercialization- of the seed sector. Its experience in technology transfer would be a
particularly valuable contribution in the areas of advanced seed varieties, new seed
development techniques, good research management, efficient seed certification, modem
processing technology, effective quality control and appropriate effluent treatment. The
Bank Group's experience in commercialization in many countries in the areas of
enterprise govemance, market liberalization, private sector development, regulatory
development, reform sequencing, legal restructuring, financial development, and efficient
business practice would assist in the implementation of the project. Indeed, project
preparation itself has already assisted project staff and policy-makers in leaming about
development of commercial seed industries of other countries through seminars and a
study tour. It also facilitated the opening of links with multinational seed enterprises,
which may lead to possible joint ventures.
F. DESIGN CONSIDERATIONS
2.37  Modernization of the seed sector and the development of a commercial focus will
be ineffective without policy reform at all levels of government. The central government



- 20 -
is committecl to developing a modem seed sector and has already begun to introduce a
number of policy reforms with respect to the liberalization of seed prices and the
separation of quality control from seed production. These are encouraging developments,
which began with the implementation of the ASSP, and need to be further developed and
consolidated. This proposed project would provide support for the separation of seed
quality management systems in four provinces (Guizhou, Shandong, Shanxi and
Zhejiang) not supported under the ASSP. This is essential if seed quality control is to be
improved in China. The project in addition would support a number of other policy
reformrs, in particular the liberalization of trade, the revision of the seed laws, the
development and implementation of plant breeders rights protection, the use of modem
ii'ternational standards for seed testing, the revision of the crop varietal evaluation and
registration system, and the development of a voluntary system of seed certification for
use in iaitemational trade in seed. All of these developments are under active review by
the authorities in China.
2.3R   China s seed sector is in a transitional stage. As described in para. 2.17, seed
compariaes are still state owned enterprises (SOEs). The government is seriously
discussing overall state enterprise reform and such general reforms will affect seed
enterprises. State-owned seed enterprises are also subject to their own regime of controls
and guidance. Seed pricing and trade are still not fully liberalized for certain strategic
crops, and only vegetable and horticulture seeds are fully liberalized. Under these
circumstances, profitable seed enterprises have been able to develop a mix of activities,
augmenting production of less profitable conventional grain seeds with production of
more profitable hybrid grain seeds and those sold in liberalized vegetable and horticulture
markets. Since it is likely that the seed sector will still not be fully liberalized within the
next three years, the overall rationale for the project is therefore the development of the
foundations of a commercial seed industry by modernizing those forward looking seed
enterprises that could subsequently become the leading companies in a national seed
industry, after SOE reforms and fuller liberalization of prices and trade in the seed
industry have taken effect.
2.39   Stated in other terms, the project aims to commercialize the seed sector, not
necessarily to privatize it. Commercialization under the project would increase efficiency
by improving management and creating incentives for seed enterprises to behave like
seed enterprises in other countries. All companies would be required to provide an
opening balance sheet with all items, including transferred equity valued according to the
1993 accounting standards.
2.40   The project would not directly address the social responsibilities of the seed
companies.  Project companies would not provide housing, but rather compensate
workers with. higher salaries. Companies would provide for pensions and health
insurance rather than direct provision.
2.41   Seed enterprises will evolve within the framework of a facilitative national seed
sector policy. But without a program of support to evolving firms, this evolution could



- 21 -
be too slow to generate an efficient seed industry in a reasonable time frame or even to
sustain the momentum of commercialization. What is needed is a nucleus group of
active, technolugically adept, competitive seed companies. At preparation, almost 100
seed enterprises were interested in joining the project. Bank Group missions established
technical, financial and business criteria to evaluate their proposed investments, business
and marketing plans and selected 31 seed enterprises and one seed machinery firm.
These seed enterprises are located in Fujian, Gansu, Guangxi, Guizhou, Henan, Hubei,
Hunan, Jiangsu, Jiangxi, Shandong, Shanxi, Sichuan and Zhejiang. The seed machinery
firm is located in Shanghai. The selection criteria were as follows:
(a)   Technical: The proposals for the production, processing, storage and
marketing of seeds are technically feasible.  Production would meet
national quality specifications (or international specifications where
exports were proposed).
(b)   Business Risk: The proposal adequately addresses the commercial and
business risks associated with creating or expanding production, processing
and marketing operations. The enterprise would have more than one
product line and would not be reliant on untried or new products or
processes.
(c)   Funding Adequacy: The firn would provide at least 20 percent of their
oNvn equity and would have government guarantees for the repayment of the
project loan, counterpart funding and loans for working capital.
(d)   Investment Analysis: The investment and production plan would produce
sufficient financial rate of return to support the loan repayments, interest on
the loans, and dividends to shareholders, in addition to providing funds for
investnent in long tern growth, even at 80 percent production capacity or
with variation of 10 percent in production costs and selling prices.
Assurances werc obtained that subloans would be made for subprojects that are
determined, on the basis of guidelines acceptable to the Bank Group, to be technically
feasible and economically, financially and commercially viable.
2.42  In addition, eligibility criteria were established for provinces to be included in the
project so as to ensure successful implementation of the project at the provincial level.
These criteria were agreed during negotiations as follows:
(a)    Separation of extemal quality control from the seed companies, and the
provincial government guarantee of budgetary support for the external
quality control agencies after its separation (para. 3.7).
(b)   Acceptance of at least one seed company for inclusion in the project.
(c)   Execution of project implementation agreements (PIAs) with MOF and
MOA (para. 4.5).



- 22 -
3. THE PROJECT
A. PROJECT OBJECTIVES
3.1   The main objective of the project is to assist the Government in developing a
viable commercial seed sector, which can not only produce high quality seeds in
sufficient quantity and variety to satisfy farmer needs but also operates through efficient,
self-sustained commercial enterprises.
3.2   The project would assist in the commercialization of the seed sector by:
(a) helping the Government establish a seed policy and a regulatory framework that
would enhance the competitiveness of the seed sector and encourage international
cooperation, (b) supporting the modernization and rationalization of selected seed
companies, and (c) assisting in strengthening institutions so they can provide effective
service and technical support to the seed sector.
B. PROJECT COMPONENTS
3.3   The project would have three major components as follows:
(a)   Policy and Regulatory Framework;
(b)   Commercialization of Selected Seed Companies; and
(c)   Institutional Strengthening.
C. DETAILED FEATURES
Policy and Regulatory Framework
3.4   This component is designed to:
(a)   Assist the Governmenit with assessment and, where needed, strengthening of
seed laws, regulations and administrative instructions in respect of the
official examination and approval of cultivars for use, authorization for seed
production and commercialization activities, labeling and packaging
standards and strengthening of seed inspection and quarantine provisions;
and
(b)   Assist the development and implementation of a suitable legal framnework
for protection of plant breeders rights legislation and the Government's
accession to the UPOV convention on intellectual property protection for
plants.



- 23 -
3.5   MOA is in the initial phase of strengthening national seed legislation and
operating regulations and implementation protocols.  The general thrust of new
legislation has been developed and the areas to be strengthened have been identified. It is
important for the effective operation of the project, which aims to facilitate
commercialization of the seed sector, that this process be expedited and that the new
legislation is both comprehensive and facilitative of international trade in seeds. The
project would provide a few person-months of technical assistance to assist the seed law
drafting committee in reviewing technical parameters and the draft seed laws. A study
tour would be provided for four persons for two weeks during the early part of project
implementation, to study application of seed laws and seed labeling as a quality control
mechanism in seed marketing.
3.6   The government intends to develop and implement a legal framework for the
protection of intellectual property rights for plant varieties. This legal protection is
vitally important for the acquisition of new varieties by seed companies and would
buttress the commercial viability of the seed marketing sector. In addition to providing
for plant variety protection within the country, the Government has indicated its desire to
accede to the 1978 Act of the UPOV Convention to facilitate international trade and
access to improved germplasm of crop species from abroad. The legal framework will
therefore need to be compatible with the provisions of the 1978 Act of UPOV as well as
having the appropriate operatioiial mechanisms for its effective operation in China. The
prcject would provide for an international legal expert, preferably from UPOV, to assist
the technical group in China to finalize the proposed framework. It would also provide
an additional study tour for four persons for two weeks to study application of plant
breeders' right registrations.
3.7   The Government's policy is to separate internal seed quality management by seed
companies from official seed quality control by regulatory agencies (para. 2.32). In 1995,
separation was carried out in eight provinces under the ASSP; namely Liaoning,
Shanghai, Hunan, Sichuan, Guangxi, Jiangsu and Shaanxi. The other ASSP provinces,
Heilongjiang and Hebei, are expected to achieve separation by the end of 1996 upon
completion of office and laboratory buildings and the procurement of laboratory
equipment, providing remaining budget problems are solved.  Since a complete
separation is essential to further reform under this project, a condition of eligibility to
participate in the project would be that the provinces have completed the above-
mentioned separation and established a suitable, financially sustainable system of seed
quality control.
Commercialization of Selected Seed Companies
3.8   The main objective of this component would be to prepare a core group of about
31 active, technically adept and competitive seed companies as a foundation for a viable
commercial seed sector-15 seed companies and 16 state farm companies.  These
enterprises would introduce new genetic materials, improve seed quality and strengthen
their institutional, marketing and sales networks by forming liaisons with Chinese and



- 24 -
foreign institutions, suppliers and marketing agencies. The project would encourage joint
venture agreements with foreign suppliers to upgrade the seed processing equipment and
technology of the companies. A detailed description of the selected companies and a
summarv of their business plans can be found in Annex 1.
3.9   These companies would, upon completion of agreed eligibility requirements for
participation in the project, receive subloans from their respective provinces to carry out
the following activities:
(a)   Improve Company Seed Production Bases. In order to develop their
production bases, a number of the seed companies need investment in land
development, irrigation facilities, drainage, power supply and rural roads.
The project would also finance farm machinery and equipment such as
tractors, trailers and seed drills.
(b)   Improved Variety of Seed. The project would assist about half of the
companies in improving their seed stock by producing or purchasing new
genetic materials or improved varieties of seed. The project would finance
the purchase of domestic and international license agreements for new
varieties, new parent lines from research institutes for multiplication,
construction of greenhouses, development of mother-tree gardens, and
equipment for dissue culture laboratories. It would also finance a small
quantity of production inputs such as fertilizer and pesticides.
(c)   Improve Storage and Processing Facilities. Incremental production under
the project would need to have proper storage facilities and improved
processing technologies in order to increase the quality of the seeds. A
number of companies proposed to enlarge their storage areas. A few
companies proposed cold storage facilities and seed silos. Most of the
companies requested finance for construction of processing facilities,
including processing equipment, drying floors and buildings to house
processing equipment, power and boiler equipment and seed storage areas.
The processing line equipment would include intake conveyors, intake
storage bins, driers, cleaners, graders, sorters, coating machines, weighers,
packing machines, associated switchgear and control panels. The equipment
would be supplied as integrated processing lines, the design of which would
depend on the seeds to be processed. Capacities would be either 1.5 t/hr,
3 t/hr or 5 t/hr.  Some cotton seed producers require acid delinting
machinery. The investment would also include design and commissioning
costs, spare parts and chemicals for seed treatment. Two person months of
technical assistance would be required to assist in preparing technical
specifications and bidding documents for these processing equipment.
(d)   Internal Quality Control. The project would finance construction of small
laboratories and supply them with equipment for quality control according



- 25 -
to International Seed Testing Authority standards. Depending on the size of
the company, the labs would be equipped and staffed to handle between
2,000 to 5,000 samples per year.  Staff would be trained in modem
inspection procedures.
(e)   Company Management. The project would provide technical assistance to
each company in the area of management. Several companies are in the
process of consolidation and are changing the structure of their management.
The project would provide technical assistance and training in the following
management areas: management of the enterprise, financial control, seed
handling technology, and plant operation and maintenance. Joint ventures
with foreign companies would be encouraged under the project as a means
to  improve  company  management.   The  project would  finance
refurbishment of office buildings and provide vehicles, computers, office
equipment and training equipment.
(f)    Marketing and Pricing.  The project emphasizes effective marketing.
Thus, the project would provide training to managers and staff in market
analysis, sales strategies and effective pricing. The project would encourage
the establishment of sales networks and alliances with other companies in
other provinces and countries and help seed companies develop the capacity
to gather market intelligence and collect and analyze data. The project
would finance construction of sales offices, office equipment, showrooms,
storage rooms and retail stores. To improve distribution of seed, the project
would finance the procurement of trucks and other vehicles.
(g)   Initial Seed Stock. Seed stock is a major part of the materials requirement
tor the seed companies. Seed companies generally purchase seeds after
harvesting, and process and store them for six months or more until the
subsequent year's planting season. Lack of operating funds has been a
major constraint limiting quantities purchased and processed. Under this
project, there would be a need for $57.4 million to nurchase the initial stock
of seed. The project would finance 50 percent of the stock purchased. The
rest would be financed by commercial banks. Insofar as the seed stocks
embody improved genetics, the investment in such stocks constitutes an
investment in advanced technology.
(h)   Seed Machinery.  The project would support the modernization of
Shanghai Xiangmin Machinery Company, which manufactures a wide range
of agricultural machinery and seed processing equipment. The company is
well recognized as providing good seed processing equipment, including 20
sets for the Henan ADP financed by IDA (para. 2.34). The company lacks
good designs for key equipment such as air screen separators and gravity
tables. It also needs high quality machine tools, screen presses and
perforators to produce accurate drum separators and screens. The company



- 26 -
does not have computer aided design (CAD) facilities, which are now used
by most foreign suppliers to produce plant layout drawings to each client's
requirements, or computer aided manufacturing (CAM) equipment, which
translates CAD designs into shop floor manufacturing instructions.
The project would finance the construction of a new workshop, necessary
machine tools and CAD/CAM design and manufacturing equipment. To
upgrade their processing equipment technology, the best approach would be
a joint venture with a seed equipment manufacturer. Should such a joint
venture be established, the project would finance a contract for technology
transfer on a direct-contract basis.
3.10 The total base cost of the investment in this component is estimated at Y 1,390
million.
Institutional Strengthening
3.11  The project would strengthen government institutions which provide services to
the seed sector as follows:
(a)   Variety examination, release and protection:
(i)    National Crop Variety Examination and Approval Committee
Office (NCVEACO). The objective is to develop the present
NCVEACO into a variety release and protection office. The two
subcomponents to be established and developed are computerization
of variety information and establishment of a plant variety protection
implementation unit.   NCVEACO  would be provided with
additional staff. The project would provide a computer system and
computer training, other training, study tours and workshops on the
protection of new plant varieties, and a small prefabricated cold
store. The investrnent cost is estimated at Y 1.4 million.
(ii)    Provincial Crop Variety Examination and Approval Offices.
Similar interventions are planned for the four project seed
administration stations in four provinces. The cost is included in the
investment cost of the individual seed management stations.
(b)   Seed quality control and seed certification:
(i)    National Seed Testing Center (NSTC). NSTC of the National
Agricultural Technical Extension Center would be equipped and its
staff trained to initiate pilot work on management and certification of
fruit tree seedlings and ornamental plants for later introduction
throughout China. The investment cost would be Y 2.0 million.



- 27 -
(ii)    Provincial Seed Administration Stations. The project aims to
ensure the existence in the project provinces of an official
independent seed regulation and control system, including a working
"truth in labeling" mechanism and a voluntary seed certification
system. Most project provinces already have independent quality
control agencies, with assistance from the ASSP. The project would
support the establishment of such independent quality control
agencies in Guizhou, Shandong, Zhejiang and Shanxi Provinces,
which did not benefit from the ASSP in thls regard. For these
provinces, the project would support seed laboratory and office
construction, seed testing equipment, greenhouses, vehicles and
computers, and local and foreign training on field inspection,
laboratory testing and cross-check testing. The total investment cost
in the four provinces would be Y 36.1 million.
3.12  Strengthening of the Central Project Management Office (CPMO).  The
CPMO would raise part of its operating funds through management fees charged to the
seed companies. The project would support project management by providing office
equipment, training and study tours. It would also provide operating funds (not Bank-
financed) and training to carry out the supervision, monitoring and evaluation required
under the project.
Informal Seed Sector
3.13  It is claimed that about 20-25 percent of the self-pollinating, conventional seeds
needed in China are obtained from seed companies/stations, while the rest are seeds saved
by farmers for planting the next crop. If this estimate of the rate of seed replacement is
correct, it is higher in China than in other countries in the region with well developed
crop agriculture. While the rate of seed replacement for self-pollinated crops in China
appears to be at a level judged to be satisfactory by Chinese farmers and many
