professional agronomists (seed prices are quite low and there do not appear to be any
general and chronic supply shortages), a government decision has been made to increase
the seed replacement rate gradually to about 40 percent through organized seed
promotion campaigns. In fact, there is no substantial and consistent evidence in other
countries or in China that conventional self-pollinating crop replacement seeds from local
seed companies are significantly more productive than seeds of the same variety saved
on-farm for planting and therefore no evidence that there would be substantial economic
gains from the considerable effort that would be involved in these campaigns.
3.14  The project would finance relatively simple surveys and studies to provide good
data on the relative performance of farmer saved and company-supplied seeds, which
could be used by the seed companies in marketing and for guidance on the type and
degree of involvement in production/marketing of self-pollinating seeds. The surveys/
studies would be done for two years for relatively modest costs by selected provincial
seed management administrations for conventional rice and soybeans in Shandong and



- 28 -
wheat in Henan. An appropriate model for the surveys/studies would be the so-called
planter box seed surveys, i.e., samples collected from farmers as they are planting their
crop. The cost of six such surveys of three crops is estimated to be about Y 65,000.
Details can be found in Annex 2.
3.15  Pilot Program. The objective of the pilot program is to give more intense attention
to three companies receive subloans under the commercialization component of the project
and provide them with training and technical assistance to ensure successful
implementation of activities promoted under the project. The preparation team has already
given special assistance to the three pilot companies by reviewing their proposed internal
management structure and their business and marketing plans. The companies are required
to provide an opening balance sheet with all items, including transferred equity, valued
according to generally accepted accounting principles (para. 2.39). They would minimize
expenditures on social services, substituting higher wages and salaries for such things as
free housing for permanent employees and hiring temporary workers in the peak season
rather than permanent staff. They would also provide insurance plans for pensions and
health coverage, rather than providing these items directly. They would have effective
quality assurance systems, which would not only test seeds but also ensure the production
of high quality seed. Benchmarks have been established for the three pilot companies and
would be closely monitored during supervision. At the mid-term review, progress would be
evaluated and the experience gained from the pilot program shared with the other project
seed companies. Description of the pilot program can be found in Annex 3.
3.16  The three pilot companies are being developed from (a) a state farm seed
company, CD) a prefecture seed company, and (c) a provincial seed company. The
proposed operations of these companies will represent three different company structures,
all of which could be adopted by other entities pursuing the objective of
commercialization.
3. 1 7 The state farm model is based on Zhangqiu Leek Seed Company (ZLSC), a seed
business that is io be spun off from the state farm and establish itself as a limited liability
company. The company would have a Board of Directors to give guidance on all major
operating and financial issues. This company would have a licensing agreement with
ZhLtngqiu Municipal Leek Research Institute (ZMLRI), the only institute in China which
has de,-doped new varieties of leek. ZLSC would be responsible for the production and
marketing of foundation and commercial seed. The general manager of the company
would have autonomy for company management. He would be assisted by two deputies,
one being responsible for finance and marketing, the other responsible for seed
production and conditioning. The company would establish an internal quality control
system reporting directly to the management of the general manager.
3.18 The company would arrange with ZMLRI to be supplied with new, improved
germplasm on a regular basis. ZMLRI would have the responsibility of describing these
varieties and obtaining protection under plant breeder rights regulations. ZLSC would
have the responsibility of ensuring varietal purity so that the marketed product truly



- 29-
reflects the characteristics described by ZMLRI. ZLSC would pay a license fee (royalty)
to ZMLRI for the right to produce and market the products developed by the institute,
and this license would be established as a percentage of the price at which ZLSC sells its
seed. This arrangement would significantly change the marketing of seeds; ZLSC would
regularly launch new products into the market and it would have to manage "product
cycles" including the phasing out of products on the decline.
3.19 ZiBo Prefecture Seed Compoany (ZPSC), a prefecture company model, would
operate as a limited liability company. ZPSC would have a Board of Directors to monitor
and guide the actions of management. The company intends to continue operating as a
stand-alone operation but to increase its market share in hybrid com and to expand
interregional marketing of vegetable seeds. It has an in-house research program for
Chinese cabbage and recently acquired the production and marketing rights to a corn
hybrid.
3.20  In vegetable seeds, ZPSC has about 1,400 accessions of Chinese cabbage as its
proprietary germplasm pool, and its research effort is directed towards the development
of hybrid.;. While the company considers its genetic basis sufficient and its hybrids
superior as regards quality and earliness, ZPSC would change its products release policy
to regularly launch new products, as described above for Zhangqiu, and also seek
protection of new varieties under PBR. ZPSC would begin significant efforts to identify
other sources of improved germplasm nationally and intemationally (for Chinese
cabbage, com and other crops). These products would be secured for the company either
through licensing or otherwise. The company currently has production and marketing
rights for a hybrid com developed by Shandong Agricultural Research Institute.
3.21  ZPSC has already established proprietary packaging for "ZiBo Cabbage Seed,"
which clearly distinguishes it from any other source; this package also has created a brand
image for the company in the interregional market. The company would accomplish its
sales objectives by building on and expanding this brand identity. ZPSC currently sells
its corn seeds within the prefecture; the company recently concluded contractual
arrangements with 6 county seed companies to sell ZPSC corn seeds exclusively. ZPSC
would also pursue sales opportunities outside the prefecture, most likely through some of
its vegetable seed dealers. The company expects this strategy to be successful and
projects an increase in sales of corn seeds from 500 tons to 5,000 tons annually.
3.22 Guangxi Provincial Seed Company (GPSC), a provincial seed company model,
decided to convert to a limited liability stock company whose shareholders would be five
county seed companies in addition to the province. The management of the company
would be supervised by a board of directors elected by the shareholders. The company
would be managed by a general manager who would not be a member of the board; this
general manager would be assisted by three deputies. One deputy general manager would
be responsible for seed production and conditioning, one for sales and marketing, and one
for finance and administration. The departments of quality assurance and new product
evaluation will report directly to the general manager. The company would operate from



- 30 -
its central location in Nanning where it also maintains its foundation seed farm; away
from this center there will be five seed production locations and one sales office near the
Viet Nam border. They would produce hybrid rice, hybrid corn, peanuts and kenaf seeds.
3.23   The shareholders would contribute to the company the right to use certain
facilities for the production of seeds; five seed conditioning lines would be installed at
these facilities. The shareholders would also make available to GPSC certain personnel
during the harvest and conditioning season to allow the company to manage its seed crop
and to keep its permanent staff at a minimum. GPSC would have, through its own staff,
supervisory responsibility for operating and maintaining all seed production operations.
The number of shares held by each shareholder would be determined based on the value
of their contributions.
3.24  The clepartment for the evaluation of new varieties will perform  a critical
function, as none of the companies forming GPSC has any proprietary products or
research programs or holds any licenses to such products. GPSC therefore must urgently
obtain new varieties for testing from both national and international sources and evaluate
them at multiple locations throughout the province and in the interregional markets.
GPSC has been put in contact with potential international sources for new varieties and
the implementation of an accelerated testing program for such new products will be one
benchmark b) which this company will be evaluated.
D. STATUS OF PREPARATION
3.25 The Crovernment is seriously committed to the policy and regulatory reforms.
Several activities have been initiated and have estimated dates for completion. However,
the speed of change will depend on the economic and political situation of the country.
The government has concurred on the need for technical assistance, as provided for in the
project (draft TORs in Annex 8). Detailed government institutional strengthening has
been developed by the concerned institutions.
3.26  The preparation for commercializing seed companies is well-advanced.  All
participating seed enterprises have submitted feasibility reports, containing data on their
seed production, processing, storage and marketing plans. Most enterprises have also
provided preliminary designs and specifications for their administrative buildings, trading
and retail cenlers, storage buildings and processing plants.
3.27  Detailed investment schedules have been prepared, together with production and
operating cost schedules, cash flow statements, working capital computations, and
financing plans. Local government guarantees have been secured by the participating
seed enterprises for repayment of their Bank Group-financed subloans and for provision
of counterp3: funds and working capital. Each enterprise has presented audited annual
accounts to show that they have 20 percent equity available for investment in their project
component. All of these documents are satisfactory.



- 31 -
E. IMPLEMENTATION SCHEDULE
3.28  The proposed project would be implemented over a five-year period, starting from
1996 through 2001. Investment in land development, civil works and buildings would
start in year one, while niachinery and equipment delivery and installation would start in
year two. In preparation for project implementation, training for personnel of the seed
enterprises and the PMOs would be in the first two years. The seed enterprises are
projected to start incremental production in year three or year four. This scheduling is
considered reasenable, given that almost all the seed enterprises are already involved in
seed processing and have experienced staff. Seed company's key activities schedules can
be seen in Figure 2.
F. COST ESTIMATES
3.29  The total project cost, including physical and price contingencies, is estimated at
Y 1,824 million or $185.5 million at the existing initial exchange rate of Y 8.4 to $1. The
foreign exchange component is estimated at $63.8 million or 34 percent of total project
cost. The base cost estimates are computed from unit prices prevailing at the time of
appraisal in October 1995. The total costs include value added taxes and import duties of
$26.5 million or 14 percent of total project costs. Physical contingencies are estimated at
15 percent for equipment, 10 percent for materials and vehicles, 5 percent for civil works
and zero for seed stocks, working capital, technical assistance, training, management cost
and labor. Price contingencies are based on annual international price escalation rates for
all goods and services when expressed in US dollars of 3.3 percent in 1996, 2.3 percent in
1997 and 2.5 percent from 1998 to 2001. Total contingencies over the five-year period
amount to 8 percent of base costs in US dollars. When expressed in Yuan, annual price
escalation rates of 17.5 percent were used for 1996 and 8.5 percent, 7.0 percent, 6.5
percent, 6.2 percent and 6.2 percent for 1997-2001. Total contingencies amount to 26
percent of base costs expressed in Yuan.

G. FINANCING
3.30 Bank Group financing would be an IDA credit of SDR 13.8 million ($20 million
equivalent) and an IBRD Loan of $80 million. This would cover 54 percent of total
project cost. The remaining 46 percent would be financed by national and provincial
governments, commercial banks and self-raised funds. The contributions from each
source are as follows:

H. PROCUREMENT
3.31  All procurement of works and goods would be carried out in accordance with the
Bank CGroup's guidelines for procurement under IBRD Loans and IDA Credits, January
1995 and revised in January 1996, and all procurement would use China model bidding
documents, prepared in consultation with the Bank Group, and based on the Bank
Group's standard bidding documents for ICB. Procurement arrangements are detailed in
Annex 4, Table 5 and summarized in Table 3.2 on the following page.
3.32 Works. Project works consisting of civil works and buildings valued at $31.4
million, are widely dispersed in 14 provinces and would be difficult to consolidate into
larger packages. All of them are relatively small, the largest being less than $2.0 million,
and would therefore, not be of interest to foreign contractors. Approximately 81 percent
of works ($25.4 million) would be procured on the basis of National Competitive Bidding
(NCB) procedures acceptable to the Bank Group. All buildings, valued at $22.2 million,
would be procured on the basis of NCB. Building construction contracts would cover the
supply of construction materials and the construction work at each project site. Of the
civil works ($9.2 million), including land development and earth works for access road
and irrigation ditches, only 35 percent ($3.2 million) can be packaged for NCB
procurement. The remaining civil works valued at $6 million are small and widely
scattered throughout the project and will not be of interest to contractors. They would
therefore be undertaken through force account managed by county engineers in
coordination with the seed companies.
3.33  Goods. To an extent practicable, contracts for goods shall be grouped in bid
packages estimated to cost $200,000 equivalent or more each. Project goods consisting
of machinery, equipment, vehicles and materials are estimated at $61.1 million. ICB
procedures would be used for seed processing equipment ($26.2 million), truck and
utility vehicles ($4.5 million) and fertilizer ($11.0 million), valued at $41.7 million or 68
percent of all goods. Domestic manufacturers would be allowed a margin of preference
on ICB equal to 15 percent of the c.i.f. price of imported goods or the prevailing custom
duty, whichever is lower.
3.34  Small office and electrical equipment ($2.2 million), farm  machinery and
implements ($0.8 million), and other equipment ($0.7 million) would be dispersed in
location, spread over the project period and subject to continuing after-sale service
requirements to the extent that none of them can be grouped in packages of more than
$200,000 and would therefore be unlikely to be of interest to foreign suppliers without a
significant local presence. They would, therefore, normally be procured under contracts
awarded on the basis of NCB procedures acceptable to the Bank Group.

3.35  Goods that should be procured as an extension of an existing contract or must be

of direct contracting. A technology transfer package from a joint venture partner, valued
at $2.0 million, would be procured on a direct contract basis acceptable to the Bank
Group. Specialized equipment for quality control laboratories, valued at $2.5 million,
and a small amount of fertilizer costing less than $50,000 per contract and pesticides,
valued at $2.6 million, would be procured through LIB. Due to the specialized nature of
the genetic materials required under the project and the limited number of suppliers of
such materials, 77 percent ($2 million) of the procurement of germplasm and seed
licenses, valued at more than $200,000 per contract, would be procured through LIB, and
23 percent ($0.6 million), valued at less than $200,000 per contract, would be procured
through national shopping (with at least three quotations).
3.36  Of market surveys valued at $5.1 million, 70 percent ($3.6 million) would be
contracted out on an NCB basis. The remaining 30 percent ($1.5 million), composed of
small contracts for remote seed companies, valued at less than $50,000 for each contract,
would be procured by national shopping (with at least three quotations). Procurement of
very small orders and various miscellaneous materials, estimated at less than $50,000
each, valued at $0.2 million would be by national shopping. Procurement of seed stock
by the 31 seed companies over the period of three years, valued at $57.4 million, would
be by direct purchase according to the companies' production contracts with farmers,
which would be specified in their business development plans.
3.37  Training and Technical Assistance would cost $2.6 million in total, of which
$0.2 million would be for foreign-supplied expertise ($43,800 for institutional
strengthening, $123,000 for policy and $52,900 for project implementation), $1.5 million
for international training and study tours, and $0.9 million for domestic training and
research. Employment of all consultants would be in accordance with Bank Group
Guidelines for the Use of Consultants, and the Bank Group Standard Form of Contract
for consultants' services dated June 1995 would be used. Domestic and international
training and study tours would be according to annual plans acceptable to the Bank
Group.
3.38  Land acquisition and compensation for lost crops ($4.5 million), working capital
($1.1 million), recurrent costs ($0.7 million) and taxes and duties ($26.5 million), a total
of $32.8 million, would be locally funded. Passenger vehicles, valued at $0.8 million,
would be provided by the provincial governments and seed enterprises. Equipment,
training and technical assistance for the seed offices in Beijing, valued at $0.4 million,
would be provided by the Ministry of Agriculture.

percent of the civil works and buildings and about 93 percent of goods under ICB and

1. DISBURSEMENTS
3.,0  Disbursement for equipment, machinery, vehicles, licenses and materials would
be n.Tade at 100 percent of foreign expenuitures for direct imports, 100 percent of the
local, ex-factoiy cost for locally manufactured items, and 75 percent of expenditures for
other items procured locally. Disbursement against civil works would be 50 percent of
expenditures. Disbursement for seed stock wouid be 50 percent of the seed purchase.
Disbursement For training, study tours and consultant services would be at 100 percent of
expenditures.
3.41   Disbursements would be made against statements of expenditure in the case of
overseas training, study tours, and contracts for goods and works costing less than
$200,000 and consultant contracts costing less than $100,000 for firms and $50,000 for
individual. A condition of disbursement for the seed stock for each subproject would be
that operation plans for the subproject acceptable to the Bank Group have been prepared
and the subproject's seed processing lines have been installed and are ready for
operation. Disbursements for all works carried out under force account would be made
against statements of physical progress achieved at each site at unit prices agreed with the
Bank Group at the start of the project and subject to annual review. In order to facilitate
disbursement, Special Account would be opened at a commercial bank acceptable to the
Bank Group, with an initial deposit of $6.0 million, representing the average of four
months eligible expenditures. Applications for replenishment of these accounts would be
submitted monthly or when the account is drawn down to 50 percent of its initial deposit.
Retroactive finincing for training and preparation activities necessary for a smooth start-
up of the project, up to SDR 5.8 million ($8.4 million equivalent), would be made against
expenditures made between October 15, 1995 and the date of signing of the Loan/Credit
Agreement.
3.42  The project is expected to be completed by June 30, 2001, and the closing date
would be June 30, 2002. Disbursement experience in China has been favorable and better
than Bank-Group-wide and East Asia regional averages. The project disbursement
profile is essenlially in line with that for previous agricultural development and institution
building projects in China. The estimated disbursement schedule is given in Annex 4
Table 6.1.
