J. ACCOUNTS AND AUDITS
3.43  Records of expenditures on civil works, equipment and materials would be
maintained individually by the participating seed companies. The records would be
consolidated by the project provincial PMOs for review and submission to the CPMO at
MOA in Beijing. The provincial PMOs would make sure that the accounts are audited
annually and would submit the audit reports to CPMO. The latter office would be
responsible for submnitting project consolidated accounts and consolidated audit reports to
the Bank Group. Assurances were obtained at negotiations that: (a) the Special Account
would be audited annually by independent auditors acceptable to the Bank Group, (b) the
provincial PMOs would maintain consolidated accounts for all components for annual
aaditing by independent auditors acceptable to the Bank Group, (c) audited accounts for
the entire project together with the auditor's report would be submitted to the Bank
Group within six months of the close of each calendar year, and (d) the seed companies
would have their financial statements audited by independent auditors acceptable to the
Bank Group.  The consolidated audit report would contain a summary of all the
provincial audit reports and audited accounts of MOA's project expenditures. It would
include details of withdrawals from the Credit/Loan accounts made on the basis of
Statements of Expenditure and the auditor's opinion as to whether such withdrawals were
against expenditures eligible for reimbursement by IDA/IBRD.
K. LAND AcQUISITION
3.44  Land acquisition under the project by the 31 project seed companies would total
only about 39 ha, mainly commercial or unused land. They typically already own the
land designated for expansion or will contract with smallholder outgrowers to multiply
seeds on their own land. Out of 39 ha, only 1 ha is being farmed by three families. These
families would be allocated new farm land of equal quality and would get crop
coinpenisation according to applicable Chinese laws. They would not be relocated from
their houses. Details can be found in Annex 1, Table 9. Assurances were obtained at
negotiationzs that, .if any of the subprojects would involve any resettlement, it has to be
carried out according to agreed principles and procedures and, if the numbers of
primary affected persons in ary subproject reach 200, the province concerned will submit
a resettlement action plan to the Bank Group for review as a condition of eligibility for
inclusion of the subproject in the project.
L. ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACTS
3.45  The project was classified as a B category. It would not be expected to produce
any significant detrimental environmental impacts in the project areas.   Standard
environmental analysis for subprojects would be conducted by Provincial Environmental
Protection Bureaus (PEPBs), as needed, for new and existing facilities. The processing
plants have a limited potential for localized water, air and noise pollution within and
around the plant buildings, but procurement specifications for processing equipment
would maintain air emissions below the national thresholds for particulate matter, and



- 38 -
would include full environmental protection measures to maintain pollution within
national EPB standards. The project proposes to treat the effluent of all processing plants
and administrative and marketing buildings in septic tanks where necessary or, where
possible, to discharge effluent into municipal sewers. The combined effluent at the 31
project locations is estimated to be < 1,000 m3/d with BOD concentration of 350-500
ppm.
3.46  The domestic heating system boilers and seed dryers will generate ash, and it has
been standard practice to use this ash in road construction and as an extender in brick
manufacture. The disposal of the negligible volumes of ash and clinker generated by
project activities should therefore not pose any problem. The cotton delinting plants
would use dilute acid foam delinting systems that were developed in the United States to
meet EPA standards. Similar plants have been used in China since the mid-1980s.
Concentrated acid is diluted for application to the cotton seeds and reacts with cellulose
linters to produce hydrolyzed cellulose, mainly sugars, that can be used as animal feed
enhancers. The residual acidity in the processed seed is neutralized in the process with an
alkali solution. It would be a condition of eligibility for inclusion in the project that each
subproject be in compliance with environmental standards acceptable to IDA and with
Chinese regulations.  Thus, the designs and equipment specifications of the seed
processing plants would be reviewed and approved by the relevant PEPB as part of its
environmental analysis and clearance of each facility. The PEPBs would monitor that
these processing plants maintain at all times the environmental protection standards.
3.47  The improvement and expansion of seed production under the project does not
differ from crop production for the same crops, except in terms of additional labor for
greater inspection and supervision. Similarly, since the seeds and planting materials
produced and distributed would be of crops already in production with variety or
genotype being the only change, the works of the project would not significantly change
interactions between crop agriculture and the environment.  -The fertilizers and
agricultural chemicals used in the production of seeds would be standard fertilizers,
pesticides, herbicides etc., as used in major agricultural economies for similar types of
seed production. All farm chemicals procured through the project would be ICAMA-
approved products. Assurances to this effect were obtained at negotiations. Assistance
would also be required in bidding documents from chemical suppliers to provide training
of safe use, handling and storage in accordance with guidelines acceptable to the Bank
Group. Assurances to this effect were obtained at negotiations. Optimal seed coatings
and coating systems would be determined during the project with the aid of foreign
technical assistance and would be ICAMA-approved products.  Seed treatment
technologies and chemicals (pesticides) would be the same as those used in the major
industrialized countries for the same kinds of seeds. Pesticides applied to seeds usually
substitute for pesticides applied in the furrow (i.e., soil) at the time of sowing or
sprayed/dusted on young plants, and are favored environmentally, since much less
pesticide is required and its placement on the seed ensures that it is positioned where it is
needed and not scattered in the soil or atmosphere.



- 39 -
3.48   Germplasm Conservation and Genetic Diversity. China has been very diligent
since the 1950s in collecting and preserving the germplasm of its major crops. Over
200,000 accessions were assembled by 1958 after completion of a national campaign on
gerrpplasm collection begun in 1953. Some of these accessions were lost due to
inadequate and improper storage so that only 160,000 remained in 1979. A decision was
then taken to construct a proper gene bank in Beijing to be operated by the Germplasm
Resources Institute of Chinese Academy of Agricultural Sciences (CAAS). The bank
was completed in 1983 and a second was begun with support from the Rockefeller
Toundation and other sources and completed in 1986. Presently, 350,000 accessions are
it, the central gene banks, which are world-class in terms of facilities and information
processing. An additional 25 field gene banks have been organized in various areas. As
en extra precaution the entire collection is duplicated in another gene bank in a "dry"
province. Additional specialized collections of germplasm, e.g., forest species, are under
control of other institutes and ministries. The germplasm collections in China admittedly
have an cconoinic bias. Recently, however, noneconomic and endangered species in the
et -system have received attention. In the fringe areas, when new improved varieties
replacc traditional varieties, there might be loss of biodiversity. Therefore the project
would encourage participating seed companies to assist germplasm resources institutes in
collecting existing species, so as to maintain biodiversity.
3.49  The genetic diversity in crop species in China is about the same as in other
countries with a high yield agriculture. The project will have a positive effect on the
genetic diversity of the crops involved in several ways. Firstly, commercialization of the
seeds sector will increase competition among the companies, which they will address in
large part by acquiring or developing proprietary varieties, i.e., varieties that are different
from others in the market in ways perceived to be important to farmers. The result will be
a more diverse array of varieties available to farmers. Secondly, liberalization of the crop
variety examination and registration requirements and procedures will facilitate the
importation of varieties from other countries, thus increasing the diversity of varieties.
Thirdly, enactment and implementation of a legal framework for the protection of plant
variety rights, as seems likely, will provide additional strong incentives for crop
improvement, research and development and make feasible the introduction of very
important germplasm and transgenetic products.



- 40 -
4. PROJECT ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
A. PROJECT ORGANIZATION
4.1   Project organization would be quite complex, involving central and provincial
authorities. At the central level, the Ministry of Agriculture (MOA) would be responsible
for overall project implementation. MOA would assign responsibility to the Bureau of
State Farms (BSF) to oversee the implementation of activities concemned with state farm
seed companies and to the Department of Agriculture (DOA) for all other project
activities, including strengthening of DOA offices at the central level. Both the BSF and
the DOA would delegate responsibility for implementation of provincial-level activities
to participating provincial governments, which would deal directly with project seed
companies.
4.2   Project Leading Groups (PLGs). PLGs would be as small as possible to
maximize efficiency. The central PLG would be chaired by a Vice-Minister of MOA in
Beijing and the provincial PLGs by the Vice-Governors responsible for agriculture. The
members of the provincial PLGs would be the Directors of the Bureaus of Agriculture
(BOAs), the General Managers of the provincial-level BSF, the Directors of the
Provincial Academies of Agricultural Sciences, the Managers of the provincial-level
Agricultural Bank branches, and the Directors of the Bureaus of Finance. The two groups
would have representation from seed companies. PLGs at both levels would coordinate
and monitor implementation and resolve any disputes that may arise. They would have a
major role to play in the promotion, adoption and implementation of policies related to
the seed industry, and they would assume responsibility for ensuring provision of
adequate counterpart funds. At negotiations, an assurance was obtained that the PLGs
would be maintained with terms of reference, composition and resources acceptable to
the Bank Group.
4.3   Project Management Offices. Project management offices would be set up at
MOA and in participating provinces to coordinate project implementation. The Central
Project Management Office (CPMO) would be staffed with personnel from the DOA and
the BSF. It would: (a) assist the Provincial Project Management Offices (PPMOs) in
drawing up implementation schedules, (b) coordinate project activities with the line
agencies at the national and provincial levels, (c) arrange for combined procurement
under ICB or LIB, (d) coordinate overseas training, study tours and technical assistance,
(e) assist the PPMOs in withdrawing funds from the Credit/Loan, (f) ensure that
consolidated accounts, audits and progress reports are prepared and submitted to the Bank
Group on time, (g) undertake project monitoring, evaluation and supervision (para. 4.13),
and (h) arrange for the project launch workshop (para. 4.1 1), a mid-term evaluation of the
project and a completion report after the project completion (para. 4.13).



- 41 -
4.4   PPMOs would be staffed with trained and qualified personnel from both the
provincial BOAs and BSFs. The PPMOs would assist the seed enterprises in liaison with
provincial and central authorities regarding assistance needed to facilitate project
implementation. They would also coordinate project works with the CPMO. Assurance
was obtained at negotiations that the borrower would maintain and support the CPMO
and the PPMOs with terms of reference, composition and resources acceptable to the
Bank Group.
4.5  The Ministry of Finance (MOF) would be responsible for the financial
management of the project and the maintenance of consolidated financial accounts. In
order to ensure the effective implementation of the project, and confirm the terms and
conditions of relending of Loan/Credit proceeds, assurances were obtained at
negotiations that the Borrower would enter into Project Implementation Arrangements
(PIAs) with each project province. Conclusion of at least nine PIAs satisfactory to the
Bank Group would be a condition of loan/credit effectiveness.
B. PROJECT MANAGEMENT
4.6   The CPMO would assist the project provinces in liaison with design institutions
on the designs and technical specifications where identical facilities or processes are to be
p.ovided for several seed enterprises. These would include the design and specification
of standard seed enterprise administrative buildings, standard seed stores and standard
processing buildings. The CPMO would also be responsible for engaging technical
assistance to review the designs, flow charts, layouts and technical specifications of the
seed processing lines. The TOR for this technical assistance can be found in Annexi.
4.7   Seed company managements would have autonomous responsibility for managing
the seed companies, including the state farm seed companies, as the enterprises would be
established as limited liability companies or limited liability stock companies under the
Chinese Company Law. A condition of eligibility to receive a subloan would be that
enterprises would have been established in accordance with this law, that they have
established a suitable benefits system for employees, and that they be creditworthy, have
a soundfinancial structure and suitable organization and management.
4.8   Some companies, notably state farm zompanies, would produce their own breeder
seeds or parent-line seeds. Some state farm companies own and operate the farms
producing commercial seed. For most project companies, however, the production of
seed would normally be undertaken as at present by individuals contracted under the
household responsibility system. Under the project, seed growing would however be
closely supervised by technical staff from the seed companies, who would provide
extension advice. The companies would supply production inputs on credit to those
farmers unable to purchase inputs from the township agroextension centers.  The
companies would supply foundation or mother seed, together with fertilizer and
chemicals not generally available in many counties. Fields would not be subject to
routine inspection for certification, but sample fields would be inspected by staff of the



- 42 -
seed companies to monitor physical and genetic purity. Competent technicians would
also be responsible for sampling and testing all batches of seed before packing to ensure
that the technical description on the seed label is accurate.
4.9   Management of the new independent external quality control agencies will be the
responsibility cf the provincial agriculture bureaus. The present seed station staffs will
form the core staffs of these new units. The heads of the units will report to the heads of
the agriculture bureaus and not to seed companies.  Assurance was obtained at
negotiations that each project province would introduce, no later than December 31,
1996, a program of random field crop inspection and testing ofpacked seed In the event
that a field does not meet the necessary standards of uniformity they would have the
responsibility to prevent use of the crop for seed purposes. In the event that a sample of
seed failed routine tests they would immediately carry out tests on all packs from the
batch and if a sufficient number of samples failed to meet the necessary standards they
wgould prohibit sale of seed from the batch. They would also carry out tests on behalf of
farmers or groups of farmers in the event that seed fails to meet the specifications on its
p ick label. The results of these tests would be used by the farmers in pressing their case
for zompensation .-om tiie seed company.
C. FINANCIAL MANAGEMENT
4.1   MOF v. uid allocate the IBRD Loan and IDA Credit to the Provincial Bureaus of
Finance .o be onlent to the seed companies.  The interest rate from MOF to the
participating provinces would be 5.5 percent with a repayment period of 20 years,
inclusive of 5 years grace period. The participating provinces would onlend funds to
eligible seed cornpanies at an interest rate of at least 6.5 percent, with a maturity of up to
20 years, including 5 years of grace; the enterprise would bear foreign exchange risk
Assurances were obtained at negotiations to this effect.
D. PROJECT LAUNCH WORKSHOP
4.11  The project is complex and involves the introduction of radically new approaches
to doing business whereby seed companies would become financially independent units
rather than line agencies of government supplying subsidized seed to farmers. To
familiarize staff of the seed companies, BOAs, BSFs and PMOs with the project
concepts, staff of the Bank Group and local experts would conduct project launch
workshops, during the first six months after project approval, in selected provincial
capitals. The purpose of the workshops would be to: (a) train staff in the commercial
operations of seed businesses, (b) clarify the individual tasks and responsibilities of all
staff involved in the project, (c) review seed company implementation plans, and
(d) clarify the operational and reporting procedures of the Bank Group.
E. TRAINING AND TECHNICAL ASSISTANCE
4.12  The training program for the project is shown in Annex 9. Each year, the CPMO
would submit a detailed plan to the Bank Group for review before the end of the



- 43 -
preceding year. Overseas training and study tours would have to be detailed with
objectives, place, time and participants. Assurances were obtained at negotiations that
annual overseas training and study tour plans would be submitted to the Bank Group by
October 31 of the preceding year for prior approval and all consultants would be hired
in accordance with IBRD 's guidelines on the selection and use of consultants.
F. SUPERVISION, MONITORING, EVALUATION AND REPORTING
4.13  The project management at the central and provincial levels would monitor
project progress. In order to ensure smooth implementation, the CPMO would establish
procedures for project supervision. Regular supervision would be carried out to assess
the physical, technical and financial aspects of the project. Details of the supervision plan
are described in Annex 10. Each seed company has its own implementation plan and
progress would be evaluated against that plan.  Monitoring indicators, including
performance indicators, were established and would be used to evaluate progress under
the project. These are (a) completion of the draft seed and plant variety legislation;
(b) accession to UPOV and ISTA; (c) development of external quality control offices in
the project provinces; (d) successful establishment of seed companies as autonomous
commercial entities; and (e) formation of these seed companies of alliances with seed
companies in other provinces and countries. The CPMO would assist the PPMOs to
evaluate the impact of the project on agricultural production, farm incomes and selected
environmental parameters (details in Annex 7). Progress reports would be produced by
all PPMOs, summarized by the CPMO and forwarded to the Bank Group every six
nonths, by September I of each year for the preceding January-June, and by March I for
the preceding July-December. A mid-term review of the project would be undertaken by
the CPMO by June 30, 1998 to assess progress according to the key performance
indicators, and chart implementation of the project for the remaining period. The CPMO
would make a broad assessment of the project's implementation in a draft project
completion report to be submitted to the Bank Group no later than six months following
the completion of the Credit/Loan disbursement. Assurances regarding the preparation
and timing of progress and completion reports and mid-term review were obtained at
negotiations.



- 44 -
5. PRODUCTION, MARKETS AND PRICES
A. PRODUCTION
5.1   Incremental Seed Production. The project would result in about 272,600 tons
of increased seed production annually at full production (Annex.S, Table 1). The
additional seed would likely include 210,997 tons of major grains (hybrid and
conventional rice, wheat, hybrid maize and barley), 22,400 tons of oilseeds (rapeseed,
peanuts, and soybeans), 39,200 tons of cash crops (cotton, vegetables, melons and other
crops), and 480,000 seedlings (fruit and flowers). Among the grains, hybrid maize seed
would entail the largest increment (64,000 tons). Wheat seed, just behind hybrid maize,
would increase by 46,700 tons. Hybrid rice seed would increase by 48,500 tons, while
conventional rice seed would increase by 32,700 tons. Increments of cash crops would be
led by cotton seed (35,000 tons) and those of oilseeds by hybrid rapeseed (2,700 tons),
peanuts (9,700 tons) and soybean (9,900 tons). Vegetable and melon seeds in eight
provinces would increase by 4,200 tons. Barley seed would be increased by 18,600 tons.
Other seeds (kenaf, grass seeds, herb seeds and sugar beet seeds) would be increased by
4,430 tons.
5.2  Incremental Product. The main objective of the project is to increase product
yield through improved seed quality, including that of existing seed production. The
incremental product would be derived from a combination of an increase in overall crop
yields due to greater varietal purity and reduced seed requirements due to higher
germination rates. Commercially purchased seed would replace the seed previously
retained for seed by farmers from their own production, thus freeing up this quantity for
consumpticn or sale.  The incremental volume of improved seed would replace
unimproved or retained seed on about 6.3 million ha, including 1.6 million ha of hybrid
rice, 0.2 miilion ha of conventional rice, 0.3 million ha of wheat, 1.4 million ha of hybrid
maize, 0.3 million ha of rapeseeds, 0.7 million ha of cotton seeds, .05 million ha of
peanuts, 0.13 million ha of soybean and 1.4 million ha of vegetables. Incremental crop
production due to the project is expected to be 10.9 million tons (3.2 and 7.6 million tons
for grains and other crops, respectively). Detailed incremental yield and production can
be found in Annex 5, Tables 1-3.
B. MARKETS
5.3   Overall the marketing of improved seed is not expected to present major
difficulties. Restrictions on marketing previously imposed on the seed market, especially
on intercounty and interregional trade, would be removed under the policy reforms
introduced under the project. In 1994, the formal seed sector provided only 3.5 million
tons of seed, which was only 25 percent of the national seed requirement of 12.5 million



- 45 -
tons. The inciernental volume of seed and seedlings to be produced would be substantial,
but in the context of total demand within China, it would be only 1.5 percent of the total
demand for seeds. Most currently traded seed is of poor quality and is not expected to
present much competition. Grain seed other than hybrids is primarily marketed locally.
Vegetable seeds, especially specialty varieties, such as the Chinese cabbage seed of Zibo
County, are marketed in considerable amounts outside the region of production, with
prefessional traders taking an active distribution role. Cash-crop seed marketing varies
with the varieties, some, e.g., cotton, only being supplied locally and others being sold to
seed companies in other provinces.
5.4   Locally, some marketing problems may arise.  No seed companies have
undertaken any formal market research, and only a few have established marketing
arrangements with seed companies in other provinces. Almost certainly some project
companies will experience difficulties in building up their local market share and will
face unanticipated competition from other suppliers in the interprovincial market. As part
of their business development plan (para. 2.40), all seed companies have to develop
marketing strategies. Some have already started the marketing process and obtained
purchase commitments from seed companies in other provinces. One particular area of
opportunity is to sell to provinces with no local sources of high-quality seed of the
particular varieties involved but which have not imported seed in the past.
5.5   Domestic Demand. Demand for high quality seed has been growing rapidly,
reflecting increased product demand due to population and income growth and farmers
switching to imp,oved seed. The government policy of ensuring food supply and
maintaining per capita grain production of 450 kg per capita has put pressure on
increasing agricultural productivity, which can only be achieved through better seeds.
S.C   Corn is a prime example of this policy. At present, 75 percent of the corn area
already uses hybrid corn seed and must purchase seed every year. The government's
target is to increase usage to 95 percent, which would increase demand by 19 percent.
The higher quality seed produced under the project would find a ready market, since the
incremental production of 65,000 tons under the project would only be 17 percent of total
requirements.
5.7   Domestic demand for hybrid rice declined during the last few years, but has
started to pick up again this year. The decline was due to the low quality of many
varieties of hybrid rice seed, and the more discriminating purchases of higher income
consumers. The pick up is due to the improved hybrid varieties now available, including
hybrid glutinous rice, which is in high demand in the northern provinces. In general,
there is considerable room to improve hybrid rice seed to achieve higher yield, resistance
to disease and better product flavor.  With increased commercialization, the seed
enterprises under the project would need to take account of particular regional demands
for various hybrid varieties.



- 46 -
5.8    The government is also promoting the use of hybrid rice seeds and has targeted an
increase in the area under hybrid rice from 45 percent to 70 percent. The incremental
high quality hybrid rice seed under the project would only be 12 percent of the total
incremental demand for hybrid rice and marketing problems are not expected.
5.9   The demand for high quality conventional cereal seeds (rice, wheat) is expected to
be significantly increased due to the desire to increase yields. Most seed for conventional
varieties is produced by farm households for self-use, but this practice entails declining
yields as varietal purity degenerates. Farmers then purchase seed to replace their retained
seed with pure varieties and restart the cycle. Progressive farmers, now typically on a
three-year cycle are moving to a two-year cycle, and the average farmers are also
decreasing their seed replacement period.
5.10   The demand for high quality cotton seed is also expected to increase. This is due
to the greater profitability under the new higher cotton price and higher demand for
cotton lint from the textile industry. Very poor germination rates of cotton seed due to
poor seed quality is a factor in the current high seeding rates which resulted in the
shortage of cotton seed.  Farmers typically save their own seeds, but with higher
purchasing prices, better seed produces better returns and provides the incentive to invest
more in seeds. Germination of cotton seed would be improved with the acid delinting
seed produced under the project.
5.11   Vegetable production has increased substantially during the last few years due to
high market demand and prices. Vegetables are the only crop category that can be traded
freely in both crop and seed. Hybrid vegetable seed is immensely superior to retained
seed of local varieties, and demand for the hybrids is growing rapidly. In 1994, 1.2
million tons of vegetable seeds were sold for the growing area of 4.5 million ha of
vegetables. The demand for seed is expected to increase rapidly, due to an increase in the
area under cultivation, especially around cities, and an increase in investment in
greenhouse production.
5.12  Interpirovincial Trade. Theoretically, restrictions on interprovincial trade in
seed have beeii removed. However, a national seed market has not yet formed, and local
protection still. exists in some areas. Clear seed policy statements by the government
would be required to end such practices and facilitate interprovincial trade. Currently
interprovincial trade, or so-called "varietal exchange", continues to be largely on barter
terms arnong long-term clients, and seed companies lack information about market
demand and supply. Vegetable seed is an exception, in which interprovincial trade is
quite active and market information is obtained from an annual seed fair in Wuhan,
Hubei, in which more than 5,000 vegetable producers and traders participate. Factors
limiting the clevelopment of interprovincial trade in grain seed include inadequate
transportation and an administrative requirement of three years' testing by seed
companies befDre new varieties of grain seed can be introduced into a region.



- 47 -
5.13   All participating seed companies have proposed to increase their sales to
interprovincial markets, and when the new seed policies are fully implemented it is
expected that trends will be similar to those in the vegetable market. On average, 30-40
percent of the incremental production under the project would be sold to interprovincial
and international markets.
5.14   Export Market. Until recently, Chinese seed exports have been very limited and
are still small. The most extensive development has been in the vegetable seed trade
through the agency of joint ventures with intemational businesses, and exports of
vegetable seed are now significant. Chinese vegetable seed producers have become
members of the International Federation for Seed Trade (FIS).
5.15   Several factors have inhibited the development of other seed exports. China's
previous disinclination to join intemational seed organizations or meet intemational
export standards is one. For instance, grain seed exports have not become significant,
because China has not met the standards of the OECD Scheme for Seed Certification.
However, border trade with Viet Nam and Korea is active in border provinces such as
Guangxi and Liaoning. China's delay in joining the Intemational Union for the
Protection of Plant Varieties (UPOV) has also inhibited the import of germplasm for
production and export. A second factor inhibiting exports in the past has been the seed
trade monopoly of the China National Seed Corporation (CNSC). This monopoly has
now been abolished, and all seed enterprises are free to develop intemational business.
The project would encourage project seed companies to enter the intemational market.
5.16  Joint Ventures. Although there are very few joint venture seed companies in
China at present, the initial ventures in vegetable seed have shown the profitability of
international trade. A major deterrent to joint ventures has been the absence of plant
breeder's rights. The Chinese government is now focusing its attention on this issue,
which is closely related to other aspects of property rights reform. Many of the project
seed companies have expressed interest in establishing joint venture companies and
producing and marketing proprietary germplasm from foreign breeders. A number of
international seed companies are also interested in improved Chinese germplasm and
willing to negotiate with potential Chinese partners.
C. PRICES
5.1 7 Grain Prices. A three-tier system of prices still applies in grain marketing:
administered or "quota" prices for the portion of the crop that each farmer must deliver to
the State (in effect a form of taxation or rent payable for the use of the farmland),
"negotiated" prices, which apply to the part of the crop that the Government buys from
farmers over and above the quota, and free market prices. Administered prices have been
periodically adjusted in response to inflation, following a sharp increase in free market
prices in the preceding year (see Annex 5, Table 6). This led to increases in average
producer prices from 1993 to 1994: for rice (in eight selected provinces) 91 percent, for
wheat (seven provinces) 65 percent, and for corn (six provinces) 35 percent.



- 48 -
5.18  Seed Prices. The price of seeds of basic grains in the past has been set in
accordance with rigid central government rules, the price paid to growers being linked by
fixed ratios to commercial grain prices, which allowed the seed companies a minimal
markup. The ratios have in fact served more as guidance than as rules, and- in practice
actual markups have been determined by policy decisions at various national and
provincial levels, with the result that seed prices differ substantially from one province to
another. In general, the prices of processed grain seed (but not that of other crops) have
been too low to enable profitable processing. At present, although the market has been
liberalized and seed companies have the authority to adjust prices without administrative
permission, some governments continue to control seed marketing and prices. An
understanding was obtained at negotiations that the Government would continue to relax
price control and that seed companies under the project would be allowed to pursue
competitive and market-oriented pricing strategies.
5.19 Hybrid Seeds. The ratio of the price of hybrid seed to commercial grain is much
higher than that of conventional seed, reflecting the higher production cost. In normal
years, for hybrid rice, the ratio has been 6-8:1, whereas for hybrid corn, it has been 34:1.
However, when the demand for seed has been high, the ratio has jumped to 10 or more to
one. In 1994, the price of hybrid rice seed varied between 12-17 Yuan per kg in different
provinces.
5.20  Conventional Seeds. The price ratio for conventional seeds (conventional rice
and wheat) has been 1.2-1.5:1. The price ratios of other kinds of seeds, like peanuts,
cotton seed, soybean and rapeseed have also fallen in this administrative category.
5.21  Vegetable Seeds. The prices of vegetable seed are free to vary according to
rmarket supply anid demand. Prices vary from Yuan 400/kg for sweet pepper seed to Yuan
8/Kg for garlic seeds. Other vegetable prices are listed in Annex 5, Table 7.
D. FINANCIAL ANALYSIS
5.22  Financial analysis has been carried out for all selected seed companies. Costs and
benefits are derived from the individual business development plans, and are based on the
inciZmental costs and benefits of the preproject existing companies.  Costs were
separated into investment and operating costs. Investrnent costs include those of land
development, building construction, equipment, materials and incremental working
capital. Unit costs are based on the prevailing domestic prices for locally procured items
and international prices for imported items, with all costs updated to 1995 values. Unit
costs also include import duties and value added tax (VAT) on all ICB procured materials
and equipment. Locally procured items attract VAT only. Tax and duties amount to
approximately 24 percent of capital investment cost. Physical contingencies are included
as part of investment cost. Working capital estimates are based on requirements for
unprocessed seed storage, consumable materials in stock, work in progress (including
field crop production where relevant), processed and packed production in stock, and
accounts payable for one month including water, power, fuel, salaries and insurance.



- 49 -
5.23  Operating costs included fixed and variable operating costs, including charges on
borrowed working capital. Fixed operating costs include social and welfare bonuses
estimated at 11 percent of staff salaries, overhead at 2.5 percent of full production
turnover, insurance at 1.5 percent of capital investment and marketing costs at 3 percent
ef full production turnover. Annual maintenance cost is included at 2 percent of building
costs, 10 percent of vehicle costs and 3 percent of equipment costs.
5.24  Incremental benefits calculated for financial analysis are based on the price
differentials the companies receive from producing high quality seed. The margin
between the wholesale price of seed and the price that a company paid to contract farmers
is considered revenue to the company. An example of the financial analysis of a
participating seed company can been found in Annex 5, Table 9.
5.25 Financial Rate of Return (FRR). FRR was calculated, based on the above cost
and benefit assumptions for all seed companies. The FRRs ranged from 20 percent to 30
percent. The overall FRR of all subcomponents is estimated at 27 percent. A summary
of FRRs for all participating companies can be found in Annex 5, Table 8. Each
company has to produce a FRR of at least 20 percent at full operating capacity in order to
cover taxes and debt service payments and to allow growth in retained earnings of the
organization. Having a FRR of at least 20 percent was also a criterion for qualification as
a participating subproject.
5.26 Sensitivity Analysis. Sensitivity analysis was carried out for all seed enterprises
to establish the effect on FRR of changes in major variables, such as prices, investment
costs and operating costs. These variables were also tested against changes in production
volume varying from full capacity of 100 percent down to 80 percent The results of the
analysis show that all the FRRs are not very sensitive to changes in prices, costs and
production. (Details can be found in Annex 5, Table 8.)



- 50 -
6. BENEFITS, JUSTIFICATION AND RISKS
A. BENEFITS
6.1   The major benefits of this project would be threefold. At the sector level, an
environment conducive to commercial development would be developed through policy
and regulatory reforms. At the enterprise level, a core group of seed companies would
learn to develop profitable commercial business in response to domestic and international
commercial demand. At the farm level, there would be an improvement in farm
production, yield and incomes.
6.2   The reforms in seed policy would vitalize the seed sector in several ways. First,
they would facilitate the elimination of the present monopoly-ridden commercial
environment by promoting intercounty and interprovincial trade.   Second, by
streamlining the examination and registration system and authorizing seed production
outside breeding areas, they would facilitate commercial activity by expediting the
movement of new cultivars into commerce. Third, as a result of the introduction of
improved quality control procedures, they would make a major contribution to the overall
improvemenit in the standards and quality of agricultural seed produced and sold in
China. Fourth, the development and implementation of a legal framework for the
protection of plant breeder rights wouid stimulate plant breeding activities within the
country since ownership of new cultivars would be possible and institutes could set
reasonable prices for new products.
6.3   Fifth, the inflow of foreign germplasm to China would increase due to domestic
companies seeking new and improved genetic materials from abroad and foreign firms
seeking more business ventures, knowing that their property rights would be protected.
Domestic seed enterprises would also seek cooperation with international seed breeders
to combine the improved Chinese varieties with the improved foreign germplasm.
Through the broadening of the germplasm base and the establishment of research
cooperation vith foreign companies, seed companies with integrated research, production
and marketing functions would probably develop as they exist in most western countries.
6.4   Participating companies would have a chance to develop into profitable,
independent companies with clearly defined structures and ownership. The companies
would be managed by trained management teams with responsibility for planning,
production and marketing. Due to their profitability, the companies would be able to pay
taxes and retain cash flow for future expansion. The transparency of structure, ownership
and management would facilitate the flow of foreign capital into joint ventures to produce
seeds for export. International companies would also be expected to significantly



- 51 -
increase their presence in the Chinese market through variety licensing, variety
registration and breeding cooperation.
6.5   The major quantifiable benefits from the project would be in the area of
incremental production resulting from the improved quality of seeds. The introduction of
new germplasm would lead to further yield increases of crops such as hybrid rice and
corn. The latter could increase by 10-20 percent, based on yields currently achieved in
China compared with those of France and the United States. In addition, good quality
seed would reduce the existing excess seeding rates. For instance, farmers would save
about 90 kg/ha when hybrid rice seed is used instead of conventional rice and would save
5 kg/ha when improved hybrid rice seed is used instead of ordinary hybrid rice. Acid
delinted cotton seed would save about 15 kg/ha of cotton seed. Improved varieties of
wheat would reduce the seeding rate by 30 kg/ha, and improved corn would reduce the
seeding :ate by 5 kg/ha. In total, the saving from using improved hybrid seed would be
about Y 126 million ($15 million) per year for the project area.
6.6   The total incremental production would be 3.2 million tons of grains (rice, wheat,
corn, and barley), 316,000 tons of oil crops (rapeseed, peanuts and soybean), 280,000
tons of cotton and 7 million tons of vegetables. Total seed saved is estimated at 49,000
tons. The annual financial value of incremental production would be Y 10.0 billion ($1.2
billion), while the annual economic value would be Y 14.6 billion ($1.7 billion) based on
economic prices (Annex 5, Table 1). The net economic value would be Y 7.8 billion
($0.98 billion) (Annex 6, Table 8.2).
B. EMPLOYMENT AND INCOMES
6.7   At the farm level, the demand for labor for planting would be reduced due to less
seed being used. However, the demand for labor would be increased in the areas of crop
maintenance and harvesting due to higher yields. The incremental demand for labor is
assumed to be met from household laborers. Additional employment may be created for
harvesting and post harvesting activities. The seed companies would provide 1,360 new
jobs in the seed processing industry in 31 modernized seed processing plants, and I seed
machinery plant.
6.9   The project would have a positive impact on the incomes of project beneficiaries.
Farm budget analysis indicates that net income would be increased due to better yield and
production. For example, in Guangxi, the net farm income per hectare of hybrid rice
would be increased from Y 4,51b ($538) to Y 5,437 ($647), from Y 1,870 ($223) to
Y 3,073 ($365) for hybrid com and from Y 3,759 ($448) to Y 7,268 ($865) for peanuts.
Farm budget analysis for each province is summarized in Annex 6, Table 1. Farm model
analysis shows that per capita income of farmers in Guangxi would be increased from
Y 414 ($49) to Y 640 ($76) with the project, an increment of Y 226 ($27) or 35 percent.
The average per capita income of the project's farmers as a whole would be increased
from Y 580 ($69) to Y 905 ($108), an increment of Y 325 ($39) or 56 percent. Results of
farm model analysis by province are summarized in Annex 6, Table 3.



- 52-
C. COST RECOVERY
6.9   Cost recovery for this project would come from service fees and tax revenue from
the seed companies and incremental crop production. Fees would be established for seed
registration, certification and testing services. Assurance was obtained at negotiations
that the Borrower would develop, no later than December 31, 1997, a fee system for
variety testing and registration, field inspection and seed testing to cover the cost of these
services. Such system would be introduced no later than June 30, 1998 in at least six
Project Provinces, with a view to introducing such systems nationwide no later than
June 30, 2000. A draft of the proposal would be reviewed at the project launch
workshop. The cost of external quality control, research and extension would be
recovered through the 5 percent provincial crop tax which would be imposed on the
incremental crop production under the project.
6.10 The government would receive incremental tax revenue from the VAT and the
corporation tax levy by the central government and City Construction Tax levied locally.
The companies would generate total annual tax revenue of Y 280 million of which Y 110
million would be VAT, Y 160 million corporation tax and Y 10 million city tax. The
seed companies would be responsible for repaying the principal plus interest on their
borrowings. Repayments to MOF would be guaranteed by all participating project
provinces.
D. IMPACT ON GOVERNMENT BUDGET
6.11  The total incremental recurrent budget required to keep the project seed stations
operating at the same level of efficiency following the loan disbursement period is
estimated at Y 6.5 million ($0.8 million). The percentage impact of this incremental
recurrent expenditure on the administrative budgets of the 13 provinces is very small.
E. ECONOMIc ANALYSIS
6.12  Economic analysis has been carried out for each province and for the project as a
whole. As described in paras. 6.5-6.6, the economic benefits of the project would come
from: (a) the net value of incremental grain production resulting from better varieties of
seed, and (b) the saving of seed from the use of higher quality seed at recommended
seeding rates.  The incremental economic benefit is estimated from per hectare
incremental production based on farm budget analysis of the future situation with and
without the project, using economic prices for inputs and outputs (Annex.6, Table 7).
The net economic value of annual potential incremental production would be
Y 7.8 billion per annum, on the assumption that 100 percent of the farmers using
improved seeds in the project area would achieve the expected incremental yield.
6.13  The total hectarage for each crop in each province is estimated on the basis of
incremental seed production under the project. The incremental economic values by
province are shown in An= 6, Table 8. Project costs include all project investment
costs for seed companies and seed stations (excluding the MOA project office and the



- 53 -
Shanghai Xiangmin Machinery Company) and the fixed and variable operating costs of
the seed companies, the incremental recurrent cost of the seed stations, the incremental
farm production costs and an allowance for physical contingencies. Buildings are
assumed to last 20 years and vehicles and equipment 7-8 years. Recurrent costs are
assumed to continue at the same level for the remainder of the 20-year period as during
project Year 6.
6.14  World price projections or average export prices actually realized by China in
1995 were used to estimate constant farm-gate economic prices for traded inputs and
outputs (Aanex 6, Table 5). Economic prices for nontraded goods were estimated using
conversion factors (Annex 6, Table 6) based on the analysis of the deviations of social
opportunity costs from the actual financial prices prevailing in China, which are caused
by taxes and price distortions. All economic values were converted to local currency at
the prevailing official exchange rate of Y 8.4 to $1.
6.15  A conservative approach was made in claiming the benefits of the project. In
general, a concentrated area development effort would be needed to realize the full
benefits of an agricultural project. Since seed production under the project would be used
in extensive areas, it is unlikely that the full incremental benefit would be totally achieved
without such efforts. For instance, farmers may not have access to the inputs or support
services that would be necessary to fully exploit the benefit of improved seeds.
Furthermore, it is difficult to disaggregate the result of improved seeds from that of
improved irrigation, input supply, marketing and credit. Hence, for this economic
analysis, benefits attributed to improved seed are assumed to be only 25 percent of the net
potential incremental value of production. Based on the above, the economic rates of
return of investment in each province range from 18 percent to 77 percent. The project's
economic rate of return is estimated at 42 percent, with a net present value of Y 1.7
billion ($205 million). A summary of the analysis can be found in Annex 6, Table 9.
6.16  Sensitivity analysis has been carried out to evaluate the sensitivity of the
project's ERR to price and cost variations. The project is not very sensitive to an increase
in investment and operating costs; if the investment costs increased by 10 percent, the
ERR would be reduced to 39 percent, and if the operating cost increased by 10 percent,
ERR would be reduced to 30 percent. Labor cost has a very small impact on the ERR; 10
percent and 20 percent increases in labor costs would reduce ERR to 41 percent and 40
percent, respectively. However, the ERR is sensitive to prices of output: if price falls by
10 percent, ERR would be reduced to 24 percent.
6.17  Sensitivity analysis has also been carried out to identify the minimum incremental
value of production due to seeds that would still result in a satisfactory ERR. Usage
would have to drop to a very low 20 percent of farmers to bring the ERR down to 12
percent, the opportunity cost of capital. If 30 percent of farmers achieved the expected
yields, the ERR would increase to 74 percent. A summary of the sensitivity analysis can
be found in Annex 6, Tables 10-16.



- 54 -
F. RisKs
6.18  A risk with respect to the policy framework would be that not all of the measures
under the draft seed sector policy would be approved by the Government. This is
lessened by the open reiteration of this policy in several high-level speeches and the
initiation of several key activities to achieve this policy by the Government. Seed sector
modernization and commercialization is also the main thrust in the new government's
Seed Industrialization Program. In the project, seed companies are being restructured to
be independent companies under the Chinese Company Law. Policy actions to separate
seed administration from seed commercialization have already been achieved in more
than 10 provinces. The new seed law and a legal framework for plant variety protection
is being drafted. And, finally, the price of seed is now set according to market conditions,
and interprovincial trade for seed has become significant.
6.19  A risk with respect to the legal framework would be a significant delay in
enacting the seed law and the legal framework for plant variety protection. This is
lessened by the strong government commitment to seed commercialization and the desire
to enter the international seed trade. The first drafts of the seed law and the legal
framework for plant variety protection are being refined and are expected to be submitted
to the State Council by the end of this year. The risk of poor regulatory follow-through in
implementing the law and regulation is lessened by the involvement of provincial
governments in the drafting and the government's plan to draft implementing particulars.
6.20  The risk that no effective, profitable seed companies will emerge or they will
decline to compete outside of traditional boundaries is reduced by the selection process
already undertaken. It would be further reduced by the improved business environment
and by technical assistance to firms in business practice and management. The risk of
ineffective financial management would be minimized by training and institutional
development financed under the project. The risk of nonavailability of new superior
products would be minimized by the ability to access new genetic materials via
international trade. Production risk is very small since seed has been produced for
several millennia in China.



- 55 -
7. AGREEMENTS AND RECOMMENDATION
7.1   At negotiations, assurances were obtained from the Government that:
(a)   The participating provinces would onlend funds to eligible seed companies
at an interest rate of at least 6.5 percent, with a maturity of 20 years,
including 5 years' grace; the enterprise would bear the foreign exchange
risk. Subloans would only be made to enterprises and to subprojects that
meet the agreed eligibility criteria (paras. 2.41, 3.44, 3.47, 4.7 and 4.10);
(b)   Project Implementation Arrangements (PIAs) would be concluded between
the Borrower and the 14 provinces when they (i) have separated the quality
control functions from their seed companies and (ii) propose to make at least
one eligible subloan to a seed company (para. 2.42);
(c)   A condition of disbursement for the seed stock under a given subproject
would be that operation plans for the subproject acceptable to the Bank
Group have been prepared and the subproject's seed processing lines are
installed and ready for operation (para. 3.41);
(d)   Special account(s) would be audited annually by independent auditors
acceptable to the Bank Group, the provincial PMOs would maintain
consolidated accounts for all components for annual auditing by
independent auditors acceptable to the Bank Group, audited accounts
together with the auditor's report would be submitted to the Bank Group
within six months of the close of each year, and the seed companies would
have their financial statements audited by independent auditors acceptable to
the Bank Group (para. 3.43);
(e)   If any of the subprojects have any resettlement, it has to be carried out
according to principles and procedures acceptable to the Bank Group and,
if the numbers of primary affected persons in any subproject reach 200,
the province concerned will submit a resettlement action plan to the Bank
Group for review as a condition of eligibility for inclusion in the project
(para. 3.44);
(f)    All farm and seed coating chemicals procured through the project would be
ICAMA-approved products. Chemical use, handling and storage would be
according to guidelines acceptable to the Bank Group (para. 3.47);
(g)   The PI,Gs would be maintained with terms of reference, composition and
resources acceptable to the Bank Group (para. 4.2);



- 56 -
(h)   It would maintain and support the CPMO and the PPMOs with terms of
reference, composition and resources acceptable to the Bank Group (para.
4.4);
(i)    Each project province would introduce, no later than December 31, 1996, a
program of random field crop inspection and testing of packed seed (para.
4.9);
(j)    Annual oveiseas training and study tour plans would be submitted to the
I-P,-k Group foi prior approval and all consultants would be hired in
accordance with IBRD's guidelines on the selection and use of consultants
(para. 4.12);
(k)   Progress reports would be produced by all PPMOs, summarized by the
CPMO and forwarded to the Bank Group every six months. A mid-term
review of the project would be undertaken by the CPMO by June 30, 1998
to assess progress and chart implementation of the project for the remaining
period (para. 4.13); and
(1)    The Borrower would establish, no later than December 31, 1997, a fee
system for variety testing and registration, field inspection and seed testing
to cover the cost of these services. Such system would be introduced no
later than June 30, 1998 in at least six Project Provinces, with a view to
introducing such system nationwide no later than June 30, 2000 (para. 6.9).
7.2   Condition  of Effectiveness.   The  Borrower would enter into  Project
Implementation Arrangements (PIAs) with at least nine project provinces satisfactory to the
Bank Group as a condition of Loan/Credit effectiveness (para. 4.5).
7.3   Subject to the above conditions, the proposed project would constitute a suitable
basis for an IBRD loan of $80 million with 20 years' maturing including 5 years of grace
at the Bank's standard interest rate for LIBOR-based US dollar single currency loans, and
an IDA credit of SDR 13.8 million, equivalent to $20 million, at IDA's standard terms
with a 35-year maturity, to the People's Republic of China.

CHINA
SEED SECTOR COMMERCIALIZATION PROJECT
Description of Seed Enterprises.
1.        The project would support the participation of 31 seed enterprises
(SEs) under the project, in thirteen provinces, and would support proposals for
upgradir,g production facilities at Shanghai Seed Machinery Company, a seed
processing equipment manufacturer in Shanghai. Also the proposed joint venture
or license agreement between Shanghai Seed Machinery Company and a well
established Danish seed machinery manufacturer.
2.        The  project  would  provide  financial  and  technical  assistance,
including training to the seed enterprises, and the Shanghai Xiangmin Seed
Machinery  Company.    Financial  assistance would  cover  the  procurement  of
materials, equipment and technical services required to upgrade and expand the
existing seed processing and storage facilities, or to build new facilities. Also
the construction of administrative and marketing infrastructure required to
manage the incremental quantities of seed produced under the project. Technical
assistance and training would focus on improved management of the enterprises,
financial, control, seed handling technology, plant operation and maintenance, and
on marketing and sales.
Selected Pro_ect Participants.

production of seeds before storage and processing. The financial analysis tables
take into account storage and processing losses, and segregate the seeds into
Grade-1 and Grade-2 and trash, where relevant. See the individual company tables
in Annex-4 (Table-4) for planned sales volumes in each grade.
Gansu Huanqxua State Farm.
4.        This company would produce and market 12,250 tons/year of seeds of
which 7,000 t/y would be barley seeds, 2,040 t/y wheat, 1,400 t/y Hybrid Maize,
1,660 t/y watermelon, and 150 t/y hybrid rape seeds. The company would provide
employment for 96 permanent staff at their offices, laboratories and processing
facilities, in addition to seasonal labor at the processing plant and farmers at

Processing equipment would include two 3t/h seed processing lines and a lt/h
processing line with three packing lines for jute sacks and polypacks. Offices
would be provided with office equipment, computers, training equipment and
internal quality control facilities. The project would also provide 3 vehicles,
1,000 tons of fertilizer and 38 tons of farm chemicals. License agreements would

Shanghai: Xianamin Machinery Companv.
89.       This enterprise is a State Farm company that manufactures a wide range
of agricultural machinery and seed processing equipment. The company has provided
seed processing lines to 20 county seed companies under the World Bank financed
Henan ADP. Their existing equipment range is well constructed, and the company
offers an excellent service to their clients including installation, training,
and servicing contracts.
90.       Their equipment designs and manufacturing procedures lack some of the
technical advantages of top class imported equipment. In particular the company
lacks good designs for key equipment such as air screen separators and gravity
tables that form the basis of good seed separating systems. They also do not have
the required high quality machine tools, screen presses and perforators to
produce accurate drum separators and screens.  The mechanical "doffers" that
clean the air screens during operation in their present range of seed separators
are far less efficient than the latest rubber ball cleaners now used in imported
equipment.
91.       Gravity  separator tables produced by this company also need an
improved control system to gain the accuracy and reliability of foreign machines.
The company does not have computer aided design (CAD) facilities that are now
used by most suppliers to produce plant layout drawings to each clients
requirements, or the computer aided manufacturing (CAM) equipment that translates
the CAD designs into shop floor manufacturing instructions.
92.       The company has approached several major European seed equipment
manufacturers for either a joint venture agreement or a license agreement that
would enable the company to upgrade its seed processing machinery to the latest
technology. They have received a interest from a number of companies that might
license equipment manufacture to the Shanghai Company, or would use the company
as sales and service agents.
93.       The Shanghai company would therefore invest under the project in
construction of a new workshop, the necessary machine tools, CAD/CAM design and
manufacturing equipment and in one or more technical collaboration agreements to
upgrade their seed processing equipment technology and production facilities.
This component of the project is an important step in assisting the China seeds



Annex 1
- 69 -                               Page 13 of 17
sector to progress in terms of processing system technology, and would have a
significant long term effect on the seeds sector.
94.       The capital investment would be Y44.9 million excluding contingencies,
